==PAGE:3==
Abstract
Large modern and unconventional buildings are suggested to become more dangerous and
difficult for firefighters to operate within. To enable quantitative analysis and assessment
of building performance with respect to intervention activities, the Fire Brigade Intervention
Model (FBIM) is investigated and sought validated for application in Danish context. The
validation is performed on two scales. First, an investigation of the dataset, which FBIM relies
on, by gathering new and corresponding data of Danish origin and compare to the existing data
sets. This way, the data representing distinct firefighting activities is validated and assessed
individually. Secondly, by investigations of a fire scenario and the associated intervention
process, compared to corresponding FBIM simulations, the validity of the overall model is
validated and assessed.
To that end, a common and basic paradigm between the disciplines of Danish fire safety
engineering and Danish Fire and Rescue Services is established and related to the use of
FBIM in a Danish setting. Thus, the experimental scope of this project could be identified,
by which four experiments, containing 27 firefighting activities for individual validation and
assessment and one fire scenario with eight distinct milestones, have been devised and the
results documented.
The results indicate that, at present, FBIM cannot be validated in a Danish setting, and
the data it relies on cannot be assumed directly applicable. However, the results indicate a
significant potential for FBIM, and with more investigations and experiments it is considered
that FBIM could make a substantial contribution to Danish fire safety.

==PAGE:8==
Chapter 1
Introduction
Any person has a relationship to fire. It might be limited to the cozy and comfortable associations
of candlelight or fireplaces, or entail more horrific associations with wildfires, compartment
fires, or even wind-driven city fires. In any case, the combustion process known as fire has had
a tremendous impact on human existence. Throughout history, the domestication of fire has
influenced many aspects of our civilisation and evolution. It has been a religious and spiritual
figure. It has provided light, warmth, and protection when needed. It has changed warfare and
caused much devastation. As such, the relationship has always been two-folded, as in the old
Chinese saying: Fire is a good servant, but a hard master” [4]. The exploitation of fire has
brought many benefits, but it still happens that we, from time to time, are faced with its great
destructive power. It seems enviable that a fire will occur, which has embedded a constant risk
into our society. A risk that is managed by fire and rescue services and fire safety legislation,
relying heavily on manpower, experience, tradition, and engineering. In Denmark, based on the
last ten years, there occurs an average of 62 fires per year, involving the loss of human life
[5]. In 2022 alone, there were above 10.000 dispatches of the fire and rescue services involving
fire [6]. As fires seem impossible to mitigate entirely, it suggests that fire safety involves both
the fire safety engineering, reflected in the legislation, and the fire and rescue services. Their
common ground being to ensure optimal conditions for the intervention processes to mitigate
the damage, if a fire should occur.
In modern metropolises, the increasing need for large and effective infrastructural buildings
poses a major challenge to fire safety. According to [7], there is evidence, indicating that it is
becoming more difficult for firefighters to operate in these large and modern buildings, which
are often unconventional in design, size, and complexity. There are even indications of the
potential for modern buildings to be more dangerous than historic and conventional buildings.
In conventional building projects, fire safety is verified and ensured by standardised prescribed
codes, mostly based on historical experience, i.e. what is known to be safe enough. However,
when building projects exceed the limitations of said codes by e.g.
a desire to optimise
the design, the fire safety needs to be verified using an alternative approach, referred to as
a performance-based approach. The performance-based approach permits a computational
verification and has been developed and implemented in Danish fire safety design since 1995,
while international use began in the 1980s [4]. In order to manage and evaluate the complex
nature of the fire safety performance of a structure, the performance-based calculations are
based on empirical evidence, simplified models, or computational simulations such as CFD
simulations.
Currently, the performance-based approach in Denmark consists of a series of criteria with respect
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
1
==PAGE:9==
to fire development, applied either to evaluate the structural reliability in terms of fire resistances,
or the safety and evacuation of the occupants in terms of exposure and escape capabilities.
The criteria and verification methods are described in the current Building Regulation, BR18
[1]. Besides numerous prescriptive guidelines, there are no means to quantitatively evaluate nor
ensure the performance of a building with respect to the interventions made by the fire and
rescue services.
The two disciplines of fire safety engineering and fire and rescue services have historically been
two rather separate and distinct disciplines, regardless of their strong correlation. However, as
the performance-based approach made its entrance into the world of fire safety engineering, the
Australian and New Zealand National Council for Fire and Emergency Services chose to include
the fire and rescue services in the development of their performance-based regulation. This
led to the development of the ’Fire Brigade Intervention Method’, allowing to quantitatively
model the intervention processes and thus, incorporate the fire and rescue services into the
performance-based approach in building designs [2]. This model is a great attempt to unify the
two disciplines, enabling closer cooperation, and thus safer buildings for both occupants and
firefighters.
Project Scope
This study aims to investigate the validity of FBIM in a Danish context. The validity of FBIM
is sought to be evaluated and assessed on two different scales. First, by an investigation of the
backbone of FBIM, the dataset provided in [3], which it heavily depends on. By gathering new
and corresponding data of Danish origin and compare to the existing datasets, the validity of
the individual sub-processes can be evaluated and assessed. Secondly, by executing a larger
intervention process and comparing with corresponding FBIM simulations, the validity of the
overall model, provided in [2], can be evaluated and assessed. As an additional aim, a digital
representation of FBIM is sought developed, enabling fast, effective, and transparent FBIM
simulations.
Ultimately, validation of FBIM requires empirical data from several large-scale drills or incidents
to be analysed and compared with FBIM simulations. However, due to a lack of detailed logs
and reports of incidents or reluctance to execute large drills, this is a cumbersome affair. Thus,
this investigation is regarded as a preliminary step towards enabling FBIM simulations of Danish
fire interventions while achieving closer cooperation and a common understanding between
engineers and fire and rescue services.
From an engineering perspective, the utilisation of a method to model the brigade interventions
is expected to grant a deeper understanding of the intervention processes, enabling optimisation
of the building design with respect to intervention performance. From the perspective of the
fire and rescue services, it is expected that the method can be used to compare different
intervention tactics and thus, permits a new quantitatively temporal dimension to tactical
assessment and evaluation.
Furthermore, if this method is carefully implemented, it is expected that the method would allow
the simulation of all imaginable full-scale scenarios without the firefighters leaving the stations,
and the buildings in question being closed off for the drill. This can be a huge advantage,
as full-scale drills are expensive in time and resources, and are very case-specific. Therefore,
besides the crucial training and education of the fire and rescue services, and other involved
parties, such drills have limited outcomes in terms of identifying challenges and cannot be
performed during the design process. Hence, the implementation of FBIM in Danish fire safety
engineering and firefighting is expected to further enhance the level of fire safety by ensuring
2
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:10==
an effective, safe, and optimal intervention, promoted by the building design, while cultivating
information-based decisions and education of both firefighters and engineers.
It is acknowledged that a real intervention process is fundamentally a complex and highly
volatile affair due to the fact that a fire scenario can change rapidly and unpredictably. The
intervention process depends on numerous factors and how these factors are interpreted by the
fire and rescue services, in a chaotic and stressful environment. The usefulness of a model
such as FBIM, is therefore, highly dependent on the required level of detail, which in turn
depends on the goal of the model. Since it is extremely difficult to capture all nuances and
details of a real intervention process, a certain line must be defined such that the required
level of detail is sufficiently low to be practical while sufficiently high to allow for realistic and
insightful simulations. This requirement is kept in mind in the development of this thesis and
in the suggested adjustments to the original FBIM.
Project Composition
This thesis is composed of several chapters, documenting each step along the way, from the
project scope to the conclusion. Chapter 2 encompasses all the required theory. In section 2.1
a common ground or paradigm between the disciplines of fire safety engineering and fire and
rescue services is established. Section 2.2 introduces FBIM, and lays the framework for utilising
and evaluating FBIM to simulate fire scenarios. These sections allow a Danish perspective
to assess FBIM. In Chapter 3, the methods of this project are presented. In Section 3.1, the
experimental scope is identified, from which four experiments were devised as presented in
Section 3.2. To analyse the measurements of the experiments, Section 3.3 presents the methods
from which the final results, is derived. The two sections 3.4 and 3.5, present the developed
FBIM software and its subsequent use to run the FBIM simulations of this project. Chapter
4, presents all the obtained results of this project. In Section 4.1, the results of the data
collection are presented while in Section 4.2, the results of the model validation are presented.
A discussion of the obtained results is provided in Chapter 5, while the conclusion is provided in
Chapter 6. For future reference, a brief discussion of suggestions for future studies is provided
in Chapter 7.
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design

==PAGE:11==
Chapter 2
Theory
This chapter is devoted to presenting the fundamental theory, and the prerequisites from which
this project rests. In this project, fire safety entails both fire safety engineering and fire brigade
intervention. Therefore, this chapter aims to provide a basic baseline for a common paradigm
between the disciplines of fire safety engineering and fire and rescue services, in Denmark.
Thus, Section 2.1 serves to introduce the basic principles behind Danish fire safety engineering
and Danish firefighting, and establish a framework and perspective from which FBIM can be
assessed. FBIM is consequently described in Section 2.1, in which the model and the data,
provided in [2] and [3], are presented.
2.1
Fire Safety In Denmark
In this section, an introduction to the holistic subject of Danish fire safety is provided. The
aim is to present and outline the main principles of fire safety while establishing a common
ground for Danish FBIM simulation. Therefore, Section 2.1.1 serves to provide the engineering
perspective, the fire safety design requirements, and safety measures bestowed on modern
buildings. This contains the basic frame of reference, from which Danish FBIM simulations and
the results thereof need to be regarded. Similarly, Section 2.1.2 provides the basic principles of
Danish fire and rescue services. This serves to outline and describe the procedures attempted
to be described by FBIM, and the fundamentals to run reasonable Danish FBIM simulations.
The regulatory framework for fire safety in Denmark is defined by the three acts: the Building Act
[8], the Building Regulation, currently referred to as BR18 [1], and the emergency Act [9].The
Building Act is the governing construction law that delegates authority and responsibilities
and provides the legal framework. It refers to the Building Regulation, currently in the form
of BR18, where all the specific requirements for Danish buildings are defined. Fire safety is
mainly considered in Chapter 5 [10], but does naturally interact with numerous other chapters
such as Chapter 27 (fire classes), 29 (documentation of fire safety), etc. A guide to Chapter 5
of the current Building Regulation is also provided along with numerous appendices, which
detail the framework and methodology, and contain examples and pre-accepted solutions [11].
The Emergency Act provides the legal framework for the fire and rescue services (FRSs). It
delegates authority and responsibility, and in general terms, defines the operations of the fire
and rescue services. Together they provide the main requirements and guidelines for both fire
safety engineering and fire and rescue services in Denmark.
4
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:12==
2.1.1
Fire Safety Engineering
The basic principles of Danish fire safety engineering and the corresponding legislation are
provided and presented in this section. It aims to provide the basic perspective and context to
FBIM simulation in Denmark.
2.1.1.1
Prescriptive and Performance-based Design
The construction of buildings in Denmark is regulated by the Danish Building Act with the
municipal council as the responsible authority. Thereby, it is the municipal council that governs
and administrates all cases covered by the Building Act, elaborated in the current building
regulation [1]. Furthermore, since the introduction of a risk-based approach to FRS dimensioning
in 2002, the municipal council has also been responsible for the municipal fire and rescue service,
in terms of resource allocation and organisation [12][13].
With this risk-based approach, the dimensioning of the municipal FRSs went from a rigid
and prescriptive system to a more flexible system, permitting analytical-based decisions. The
same tendency is evident in the development of performance-based design in Danish fire safety
engineering since it was introduced in 1995 [4]. The term prescriptive measures is used for
measures that are pre-defined, indisputable, and often based on tradition and experience.
Examples of such are the pre-accepted requirements for escape routes, fire cells and -sections,
or the technical fire installations, all defined by the building topology and occupancy. If
a building or building section cannot comply with the pre-accepted solutions, e.g. due to
high occupancy or nontraditional layout, a performance-based approach can be applied. The
term performance-based refers to a method of validation by calculations and analysis of the
building’s performance during a fire. If such an analysis demonstrates compliance with the
given set of critical conditions, defined in BR18, the building is considered safe enough [4][10].
BR18 is overall predicated on performance-based criteria from which prescriptive measures for
pre-accepted solutions are derived [14].
At present, BR18 contains numerous prescriptive measures for standard pre-accepted solutions
concerning fire safety, while some performance-based validations are permitted. The main
subjects where performance-based validations are permitted are regarding evacuation and
occupant safety.
Here, fire dynamical calculations are held against evacuation times to
demonstrate that the occupants will not be exposed to critical conditions [11]. Performance-
based design has also been introduced in the context of structural fire safety. BR18 permits,
through the use of Euro-Codes, the application of a parametric fire curve to describe a fire
development within an enclosure (fire section), and thus, to estimate the fire load of the
section [10][11]. This can then be used to demonstrate the performance of structural elements
during a fire, allowing for optimal design of the structural system. In any case, the extent of
performance-based validations depends on the classification of the building [15].
2.1.1.2
Fire Classification
In Denmark, there are three classification systems for buildings concerning fire safety. These
are introduced to provide the framework for FBIM applications in Denmark.
The usage category depends on the use of the building, i.e. the occupancy, the occupants,
and their ability to evacuate. In six categories, it provides a measure to assess the expected
behavior of the occupants during an incident [10].
The risk class is defined by the usage category along with specifications regarding storey area,
max height, and whether the building is free standing or not. It consists of four classes and
represents a general level of risk associated with each building or building section [10].
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
5
==PAGE:13==
The fire class determines the method of documentation and verification and sets the requirements
for the fire consultants [10][15]. There are four fire classes which in simple terms combine the
risk class into a method of validation, as follows:
Fire classes 1 and 2: For buildings in risk classes 1 to 31, where pre-accepted solutions
are applied
Fire class 3 is used for buildings in risk classes 1-3, where both a comparative analysis
and performance-based calculations are included
Fire class 4, is for any building in risk classes 1 to 4, where special testing is further
conducted
Moreover, it is noted that any building with storey area exceeding 600m2 is at least in fire class
2, and any building in risk class 4 is predetermined to be in fire class 4.
Thus, from the use of the building, the usage category is obtained. From this and the topology
of the building, the risk class is defined. From this and the means of validation, the fire class
is given. In case of variations in either occupant category or risk class between sections of a
building, the total fire class is determined as the highest fire class among the sections.
2.1.1.3
Fire Safety of Occupants
The safety of occupants during a fire scenario is i.a. ensured by utilising passive and active
protection systems [4]. Passive fire protection systems encompass all safety measures that
have stationary effects and do not require any form of activation, i.e. the safety measures are
always present. Examples of such systems are building compartmentation, escape routes, rescue
openings, and structural redundancy. Active fire protection systems encompass all the safety
measures that require an activation prior to its effect. Examples of such systems are detection-,
sprinkler- and warning-systems, emergency lighting, smoke ventilation, fire curtains, stairwell
pressurisation, and on-site fire extinguishers. The intervention by the fire and rescue services
can be categorised as active protection, but design measures such as ensuring sufficient space
and access for the FRSs to operate and adequate systems for firefighting can be regarded as
passive protection.
When applying pre-accepted solutions for a building or section, the required protective systems
depend mainly on the usage category. A performance-based design would require a deeper
analysis to demonstrate compliance with the criteria for occupant safety. Such analysis would
rely on a risk assessment to identify critical fire scenarios, an evacuation model to simulate
the flow of occupants, a fire model to simulate the fire and smoke development within the
enclosure and models for material and structural degradation. Based on these analyses, adequate
protection systems can be identified [11].
The main aspects of Danish fire safety engineering which is related to occupant safety will be
outlined in the following. It serves to introduce the engineering perspective to fire development,
fire compartmentation, evacuation, technical installations, and structural fire safety, providing
the basis of fire safety design in Denmark.
Fire Development
The fire development of an enclosure fire is categorised by the four stages [4]: the incipient stage
with pre-heating and ignition of the fuel, the growth stage with further heating and ignition of
the fuel, the fully developed stage, where all burnable materials in the enclosure are burning,
1Fire class 2 encompasses both risk classes 2 and 3.
6
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:14==
Figure 2.1: The fire development of a general fire [16]
and the decay stage, where the fire decays due to either lack of oxygen or fuel. The phases are
illustrated in Fig. 2.1, including the three phases of transition: ignition, flashover, and decay.
Ignition represents the transition from the incipient phase to the growth phase, flashover from
the growth phase to the fully developed phase, and decay from the fully developed phase to
the decay phase.
Fire development is a key factor in any brigade intervention. The preliminary assessment by the
OIC of where a given fire is in its development is an essential component in identifying the most
efficient and safe intervention strategy, including an early requisition of additional resources [17].
For most firefighting purposes it is common to simply distinguish between the pre-flashover
and post-flashover phases, as in general, each of these poses specific risks implying different
tactical considerations. A fire that is still in the pre-FO phase is generally fuel-controlled. It
can often be managed by a swift intervention without a large water application and -supply and
is not affected by adding oxygen to the fire compartment upon entry. A fire in the post-FO
phase is generally ventilation-controlled and requires a more cautious and calculated approach
with a need for a larger water supply [17][18]. These are generalised cases and the intervention
strategies would naturally be dependent on other factors such as people and animals at risk,
the size of the fire, the fuel or type of fire, specific hazards etc. In fire safety design, the
pre-FO phase is typically related to the safety of occupants, as the evacuation is required to be
completed within this phase of the fire. Analogous, the post-FO phase is typically related to
structural fire safety, ensuring a safe intervention process, and mitigating structural failure and
collapse [4][11].
The most basic fire models, referred to as design fires, are utilised to simulate the development
of a given fire, based on the occupancy and purpose of the building or section. Thereby, there
is no need to specify all the interior, the size and openings of the section, etc., to simulate the
development of the enclosure fire. For a pre-FO fire, the most common design fire is defined as
[4][11]:
˙Q(t) = α t2
(2.1)
With ˙Q representing the Heat Release Rate (also referred to as HRR) which is a measure of
the energy output per unit time, t, released from the combustion. The parameter α is an
empirical tabular value that represents the fire growth rate. The design fire and four general
fire growth rates are illustrated in Fig. 2.2, provided by Chapter 8 in [11], which also contains
the methodology of choosing an appropriate growth rate factor along with a maximal HHR.
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
7
==PAGE:15==
Figure 2.2: Standard pre-FO design fire, Chapter 8 in [11]
Generally, the medium fire growth rate is associated with dwellings, hotels, hospitals, schools,
and offices, the fast is associated with parking facilities, while the ultra-fast is used for shops
[4]. For a fire scenario involving sprinkler systems, it is common to apply a pre-FO model to
estimate activation times.
For the post-FO case, similar approaches to model the fire development have led to the
development of several design fires. The most common are known as the standard fire and the
parametric fire, provided in [19].
Design fires are often used as a reference in fire classification of building materials and building
elements by the standard DS/EN 13501 [11]. The reaction-to-fire (RTF) rating is a classification
system of materials concerning the following combustion properties: fire contribution, smoke
production, and the development of burning droplets. This system makes it possible to restrict
and manage the use of materials according to their contribution to a fire. The fire resistance
(FR) rating is a classification system concerning the structural capacity, and the smoke and
heat permeability of the elements, i.e. the elements’ ability to contain and sustain a fire for a
specific amount of time. Together the RTF- and FR-rating systems provide the basic principles
for managing fire compartmentation and fire development within an enclosure.
Building Compartmentation
Fire compartmentation of buildings, sections, and cells is fundamental for any fire safety design
in Denmark. By §104, in BR18 [10], it is required to limit the spreading of fire and smoke
within and between fire units for the time necessary to complete evacuation and intervention.
Furthermore, it is required to limit the fire spread between buildings on the same cadastral
parcels while preventing fire spread between cadastral parcels. This leads to the definitions of
fire sections, fire cells, fire walls, and fire parapets, along with requirements for distances and
fire classifications (RTF and FR).
In general, a fire section in Denmark is classified EI 60 A2-s1,d0 and indicated with a red
color [11]. EI 60 refers to the FR-rating of the building element, indicating that the integrity
8
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:16==
concerning flame and smoke leakage (E), and adequate heat insulation (I) is ensured for at least
60 minutes relative to a design fire. A2-s1,d0 refers to the RTF-rating of the materials used in
the building element. It indicates a material composition that generates no contribution to fire
(A2), very limited smoke production (s1), and no burning droplets (d0). In some cases, e.g. for
staircases in buildings with a top floor more than 22m above ground, the fire section is required
to be classified REI 120 A2-s1,d0, now including the loadbearing capacity (R) and extending the
reference time. A fire cell in Denmark is generally classified EI 60, with no requirement for the
RTF-rating of the materials. The overall purpose of a fire section is to limit the total fire area
within a building, whereas the purpose of a fire cell is to protect and ensure evacuation. Fire
walls and fire parapet of different classifications are used to further prevent fire spread between
buildings and limit external flame spread when required. To mitigate collapse or structural
failures, the classifications of fire sections are also used to ensure structural redundancy by
restrictions to the load-bearing capacity during a fire. This ensures the firefighters’ safety while
conducting their intervention [4][11].
The building compartmentation supports the brigade intervention process in many aspects.
Ideally, it ensures that a fire within a compartment is contained for some time and thus, helps
concentrate the intervention process and mitigates rapid fire spread. The fire units next to a
unit, enclosing a fire, can, for some time, provide a safe space for the firefighters to operate
and set up. Careful exposure control can further prevent flame and fire spread between fire
units, which can minimise the extent of damage [17]. In any case, the classification of a
fire unit determines the reference time of containment, or in other words, the time at which
the firefighters have to intervene before the fire unit can be compromised. This rests on the
assumption that the integrity of the fire unit has not been compromised by improper installation,
maintenance, or supervision. Furthermore, by the purpose of fire sections, these sections
generally determine the fires which the FRSs are required to be capable of handling. The fire
sections are thus equivalent to standard fires which determines the resources available to the
FRS. This equivalence and the use of standard fires are investigated in [14], in which the term is
used to express pre-accepted fires, i.e. the fires within a fire section derived from pre-accepted
solutions in BR18.
Means Of Escape
The safety of occupants is highly dependent on the available means of escape, i.e. an escape
route at which the occupants can bring themselves to safety. An escape route can generally
be defined as a protected route to safety, i.e. outside, and on the ground [4][11]. In some
special cases, safety can be provided by carefully designed fire sections or safe rooms, but this is
rarely a preferred strategy. In general, staircases which are a part of escape routes are designed
as fire sections. This ensures the integrity of the staircase to complete the evacuation and
intervention.
The basic prescribed requirement for escape routes is the need for multiple independent routes.
This is a safety measure that ensures redundancy and flexibility for different fire scenarios. In
Denmark, for each fire cell, the minimal requirement for the number of independent escape
routes is generally two, depending on the number of occupants [11]. It should be noted that
an independent escape route is not the same as an exit. Multiple exits can lead to the same
independent escape route. Besides the number of exits and independent escape routes from
each cell, there are other prescriptive requirements such as free widths of doors, escape corridors,
total exit, the design of escape staircases, maximal escape distances within a fire cell, etc.
[4][11].
In Denmark, it is in some cases permitted to employ rescue openings to function as an escape
route [11]. Such designs rely heavily on the FRSs to perform the rescue operation either by
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
9
==PAGE:17==
Figure 2.3: General Evacuation Concept
portable ladders or ladder appliances. This is an example of how building design can introduce
bound operations in the intervention strategy and bind certain resources from the response.
When the pre-accepted solution and the prescribed requirements for the means of escape cannot
be applied, a performance-based approach is permitted. Such verification is generally a matter
of demonstrating that the occupants have escaped without being exposed to critical conditions
[4][11]. Or in other terms, that
ASET ≫RSET
or
ASET = µevac · RSET
(2.2)
Where ASET is the available safe egress time, and RSET is the required safe egress time, and
µevac > 1 is an adequate safety factor for the evacuation, as illustrated in Fig. 2.3.
In general, RSET is estimated based on an evacuation model, while ASET is estimated based
on a fire model as described in [4]. The RSET can be separated into several steps such as the
following simple case
RSET = tevac = tdetection + twarning + treaction + tmovement
(2.3)
Where tevac, is the completion time of the evacuation, tdetection is the time required for the fire
to be detected, twarning is the time required for the warning system to activate and play its
message, treaction is the occupants reaction time to the warning, and tmovement is the actual
time for the occupants to move to safety, incl. queuing. Each of these steps can be further
divided to reveal all the sub-processes within each step. For this project, the above-mentioned
case should be sufficient to understand the basic principles of an evacuation process.
Many actions can affect the estimates of either ASET or RSET. Sprinkler systems and smoke
ventilation can increase the ASET, while detection and warning systems can decrease the RSET.
It is important to recognise that both the ASET and RSET are case specific, i.e. they depend
on the specific fire scenario and the embedded assumptions of e.g. to include mobility-impaired
individuals. For more details concerning evacuation models, the reader is referred to [4][11][20].
Technical Installations
The fire safety of many buildings is supported by technical installations, which are categorised
as active protective measures. In the following, a brief presentation of the most common fire-
related technical installations and the system requirements from BR18 is given. The technical
installations serve to either increase the ASET, decrease the RSET, or support firefighting
activities.
The most basic technical installations concerning fire safety are the detection-, warning-, and
fire-alarm systems. For pre-accepted solutions cf. BR18 [11], the general requirement for
installation of these systems mainly depends on the usage category along with several specific
criteria.
10
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:18==
There are multiple ways to design each of these systems as there are many different types
on the market, each associated with distinct pros and cons [4][11]. A detection system is in
general based on either smoke alarms or fire alarms. Smoke alarms are generally the simplest
form of fire detection. It utilises a combination of detectors and acoustic emitters to notify
the occupants. Such systems are often employed in typical Danish house-holds and require
the occupants to manually notify the FRS. On the other hand, in most public or industrial
buildings (or sections), including buildings with high occupant densities, fire alarm systems
are employed. A fire alarm system, in Denmark typically referred to as an ABA system, is
usually a rather complex system. For simplicity it suffices to recognise that a fire alarm system
utilises a combination of detectors and a notification system, directly or indirectly to the FRS,
to monitor the building or section. A fire alarm system is typically connected to other technical
systems such as a warning system, a fire ventilation system, an automatic fire door closing
system, elevators, etc. [4][11]. The control panel of the fire alarm system is often located in
a Fire Central, from which it can be accessed and operated by the FRS along with other fire
safety systems. Pre-planning documents, in Denmark known as Orientation plans or O-plans,
concerning fire, are also located here.
The detection system is an important factor to consider, as early detection is vital for successful
evacuation and brigade intervention. The detection times depend on the fire and the fire
development, but also on the type of detector. In [4] and [21] some standard values for the
different detection times are provided, but it is highly recommended to consult the specific
manufacturer to obtain more accurate values.
A warning system is often employed together with a fire alarm system to alert the occupants
and initiate the evacuation. There are many different ways to alert the occupants but they are
mainly based on either acoustic or optical signals of different compositions. Ideally, a warning
system decreases the RSET as it leads to an earlier evacuation, but it is highly dependent on
the reaction and decision-making of the occupants, treaction, which is influenced by human
behavior. For example, the reaction time can be expected to be significantly lower for occupants
who can directly see flames or smoke, than for occupants who cannot directly sense or perceive
the danger [4]. This is naturally dependent on the individuals, as risk perception and tolerance
are different from individual to individual, and can be addressed by training or informational
campaigns. Many other factors of human behavior relate to an effective evacuation which will
not be considered here, but it is recognised that an effective evacuation also influences the
effectiveness of the brigade intervention in various ways. For example, if the evacuation is still
underway when the FRS arrives, the intervention activities can be delayed as the firefighters
might be traveling against the flow of evacuees. There might also be a need to manage and
support the evacuation, which implies fewer resources for actual firefighting activities [17].
Furthermore, an effective and complete evacuation can enable the FRS to concentrate their
efforts. In the same manner, buildings equipped with CCTV or similar can help scan the building
for occupants in need of rescue.
The most commonly adopted suppression system in Denmark is based on sprinklers. The
general purpose of a suppression system is to limit the fire spread and increase the ASET [4][11].
In some cases, a suppression system can also function as a detection system e.g. activated by
heat and detection of a loss within the system. There are many guidelines and requirements
for suppression systems, but for this project, it is considered sufficient that sprinklers are widely
used, depending on the usage category as illustrated in Fig. , and are primarily designed based
on the Hazard Class, to ensure sufficient suppressive capabilities. Other suppression systems
such as gas exists, but are mainly used in special cases and will for the sake of simplicity, not
be discussed in this project.
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
11
==PAGE:19==
Other technical installations which support the evacuation and are worth mentioning are fire
ventilation systems, to remove heat and smoke, stairwell pressurisation, to protect the stairwell
from smoke and secure the escape (and access) route, systems for emergency lighting, to aid
the evacuation, and automatic fire door closing systems to enforce the fire compartmentation
and mitigate fire and smoke spread between fire units. Moreover, the most common technical
installations that support the brigade intervention activities, besides the ABA systems, are
risers (either dry or wet) and firefighter elevators, each of significant importance [11]. The
risers ensure that the firefighters can perform their intervention without long and taxing hose
connections. The firefighter elevator ensures fast access for firefighters in tall buildings and
can provide the means for rescue operations. Each system can vary in complexity and type
but for the general purpose of this project, it is sufficient to recognise that many different
systems are influencing both the evacuation and the brigade intervention, which might need to
be considered when performing intervention simulations.
2.1.1.4
Design Requirements for Brigade Interventions
Within the current Building Regulation [10] there are provided certain design-related require-
ments regarding the brigade intervention. These are described in §126-133 and generally aim
to ensure that the FRS can conduct their rescue and intervention activities justifiably. The
design-related requirements are mainly concerned with ensuring adequate space and options for
access and rescue operations, and the need for firefighting systems e.g. risers and firefighter
elevators. The requirements are further detailed in [11].
The design choices made by the engineers to comply with these requirements are essential for
the employed intervention strategy. Thus, design decisions can introduce bound operations and
strategies to the FRS. This serves to illustrate that a common understanding and cooperation
between the two disciplines is vital for an effective intervention. It serves no one if the engineers
have developed deliberate and clever plans and systems for an effective intervention if the
firefighters are unaware, do not have the necessary skills or equipment for it to work, or only
work in theory. On the other hand, it serves no one if the firefighters have developed and
trained certain effective tactics if the engineers are unaware and do not support them in their
designs. Fire safety engineering and the fire and rescue services are two opposite sides of the
same coin, brought together in a unified effort to ensure the high standards of fire safety in
Denmark, to save lives, and to mitigate the environmental impact.
2.1.2
Fire and Rescue Services
This section serves to provide an introduction to the Danish Fire and Rescue Services (FRSs).
The aim is to provide the terminology of fire interventions and to present the different factors
and necessary information required to perform an FBIM analysis in Denmark.
The Danish FRS, covered by the Emergency Act [9] , operates on two levels: one on the
municipal level and one on the national level. Each level has different responsibilities but their
goal remains the same: to prevent, reduce, and remedy any damage or injury inflicted on human
life, property, or the environment as a consequence of accidents or disasters. The municipal
FRSs conduct the primary interventions, whereas the national FRS maintains a reserve to assist
the municipal FRS, if needed in conducting special or lengthy interventions. The national
FRS is known as DEMA (the Danish Emergency Management Agency) and is part of The
Danish Defense. DEMA is nationally distributed in four centers and its locations and resources
are politically decided. Moreover, DEMA is responsible for providing firefighting guidelines,
protocols, and educational standards. As DEMA mainly functions as additional and/or special
resources in interventions, it will not be discussed further. However, the firefighting literature
12
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:20==
provided by DEMA will serve as a reference for the firefighting tactics and the upcoming FBIM
simulations.
2.1.2.1
Dimensions of Fire and Rescue Services
In 2002, a risk-based approach to the dimensioning of the municipal FRSs was enacted by a
decision in the Danish parliament [22]. Thus, the municipal FRSs were now defined by the
decisions of the municipal council, based on a risk assessment of the local risks. Before this
enactment, the municipal FRSs were defined by a series of prescribed laws, primarily depending
on the number of inhabitants of the area [22]. From this point on, an analytical component
was introduced to define the dimensions of the municipal FRSs, but it relies on the municipal
council to make informed decisions. This approach allows for optimised design of FRSs, but can
also allow the FRSs to be subjected to economic savings. At present, there are 32 municipal
FRSs in Denmark, and 19 of these are inter-municipal cooperations [23]. Each municipality is
responsible to develop and periodically reevaluate an emergency plan based on a risk assessment,
desired service level, available resources, etc. [22].
2.1.2.2
Resources of Fire and Rescue Services
Even though each municipal FRS is different, the national guidelines provided by DEMA have
served to standardise some practices. In this section, a general overview of the main resources
available for Danish fire interventions is provided.
Crew & Skills
The risk-based implementation implied that the available crew of a given FRS is defined by
the municipal council. Typically, the first response consists of one team leader and three to
seven firefighters, including drivers and excluding the incident commander [14][22]. The Danish
standard firefighting guidelines dictate that a Smoke-Helmet Team (SHT or BA-Team) consists
of two firefighters [24]. Thus, some FRSs have three available SHTs in the first response while
others are content with one SHT. The available crew of the first response has a major influence
on the intervention process and strategy and is therefore considered an important factor of any
FBIM analysis [14][22]. The parring of firefighters is mainly due to safety reasons, and it is
customarily to use such a pair to solve firefighting activities, especially for internal assignments
such as search and rescue or fire control/extinguishing. The parring also reduces the physical
loads and ensures that at least one firefighter can operate the nozzle at all times, while the
other assists [24].
Omitting the few voluntary FRSs, the municipal FRSs are either organised as full-time FRS,
part-time FRS, or something in between. While the full-time FRS usually has a one-minute
response, the part-time FRS typically has a five-minute response [14][22]. Some FRSs employ
a full-time response during the daytime while relying on a part-time response during the
after-hours and weekends. This indicates that the initial response also depends on the time of
the day of the fire.
Any operating firefighter can be assumed able to carry out any of the main intervention tasks.
This is ensured by the streamlined education and training defined by DEMA. The hierarchy of
the main standard education is as follows:
GUI - This is equivalent to basic firefighting training. It permits an FF to perform various
basic tasks on an incident site but is mainly limited to external operations [25]
FUI - This is equivalent to the main firefighting training. It permits an FF to perform all
standard firefighting tasks, including internal operations [25]
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
13
==PAGE:21==
HL 1 - This is equivalent to the basic team leader training. It permits an FF to function
as a basic team leader on an incident site [26]
HL 2 - This is equivalent to the main team leader training. It permits a team leader to
function as an officer in charge (OIC) of the initial response until an incident commander
arrives [26].
ISL 1 - This is equivalent to basic training as an incident commander (IC) or officer in
charge, responsible for the technical management of an incident [26]
ISL 2 - This is equivalent to the main training as an incident commander able to perform
interdisciplinary management of an incident [26]
The above-mentioned educations all have distinct Danish names and functions, but it is
considered sufficient to refrain from further elaboration. Furthermore, there exist numerous
other special courses or internal training procedures that can permit an FF to operate under
certain conditions e.g. underwater or in tunnels, or use special equipment e.g. aerial/ladder
appliances, torrents, or closed circuit breathing apparatus for long interventions. These are not
mentioned further, but does indicate that local knowledge of the individual FRS in question,
is important. It is further noted that based on the assessments and orders from the OIC
(which can be a TL, an IC, or both), it is the individual FFs or SHTs that conduct the actual
intervention activities. Thus, it is mainly the activities of the FFs or SHTs which is to be
modeled and simulated.
Appliances & Pumps
In Denmark, there exist many different fire appliances which are in general based on DS/EN
1846 [14]. The main fire appliances are summarised as follows:
Fire engine: a standard fire appliance with equipment, a small water tank (∼1600-3000L)
including a pump, high-pressure hose (HP-hose), portable ladder, foam, etc. Usually, it
is accompanied by a TL and three to four FFs.
Ladder or aerial appliances: a fire appliance with a mechanical ladder or basket, often
including a torrent. Can be used for both evacuation and fire control and is typically
accompanied by one to three FFs.
Water tender: a larger water tank (∼3000-5000L), often including a pump, an HP hose,
and a torrent. Typically accompanied by one to three FFs.
There are many variations to the appliances mentioned above and many different compositions
of appliances within each FRS. For simplicity, it is reasonable to assume that a fire engine has
at least one or two SHTs, a TL, and contains all necessary tools. It can supply water for initial
firefighting but requires a connection to a water source for long-term firefighting. If available,
a ladder or aerial appliance is assumed to be a mechanical tool, operated by its driver. A water
tender, operated by its driver, can to some extent supply the fire engine with water for a limited,
but longer amount of time. Long hose connections to e.g. hydrants can be established while
the intervention process is well underway.
The composition of the initial responses varies from FRS to FRS and from call-out to call-out,
but is typically predefined by a so-called ’Pick-list’. This list states which units and appliances
are called for, given the nature of the specific notification [22]. For instance, it is typical to
distinguish between a fire in a villa and a fire in a garbage bin. In this way, it is ensured that
the amount of available resources in the initial response corresponds to the fire scene. This
14
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:22==
method requires information regarding the fire development and fire scene to accompany the
fire brigade notification, which might be challenging.
As mentioned, the typical fire engines and water tenders contain an appliance pump to be used
for intervention activities. These pumps can be very different but are mainly based on EN 1028
[14], implying a general water performance of either 2000 or 3000 L/min at 15 bar.
Water Capacities
The municipal council is, cf. the Emergency Act, responsible for ensuring adequate water
supply for firefighting purposes, as a part of the risk-based dimensioning of the FRS ˙Thus, the
water capacity brought forth by the initial response can be different, depending on the defined
strategy for water supply in the municipal emergency plan [14]. In general, there are two main
strategies for water supply, either based on the supply from a well-established hydrant system
or based on the supply from water tenders. In some instances, the water supply is ensured by
so-called hose-tenders, containing hoses for long connections and smaller mobile pumps used
for serial pumping and/or suction from static reservoirs. Such a strategy is mainly deployed
in rural regions of Denmark. The hydrant system in Denmark is rather well-established why
this is the chosen strategy in most cases. Furthermore, each hydrant is registered in the digital
system GIS, making the position of the hydrant easily readable [27].
Fire Equipment, Hoses & Nozzles
A standard fire engine contains all necessary tools, required for firefighting or rescue purposes.
This includes e.g. tools for forced entry, tools for freeing trapped individuals, smoke ventilators,
portable ladders, safety equipment, as well as hoses and equipment for different kinds of
firefighting strategies. Much of the equipment on a fire engine can be different from FRS to
FRS in model, size, weight, etc., but can be assumed capable of solving many of the same
tasks. In general, a fire engine can be considered as the basic multi-tool in the OIC’s toolbox.
Even though no standards are officially applied to standardise the use of hoses in Denmark, it
has become a tradition to use the following main fire hoses, inspired by the German standard
[14][28][29]:
A-hose: Ø102mm, 1200 L/min
B-hose: Ø75mm, 600 L/min
C-hose: Ø52/42mm, 200 L/min
High-pressure hose (HP-hose): Ø25-51mm, ∼115 L/min
Each type of hose is used for specific tasks. The presented water flows are conservative and
provided at what is referred to as ’normal water flow’, i.e. the flow of which the pressure loss
over 15 m of (horisontal) hose, can be set to 0.1 bar [14][29].
A standard hose is 15 m long, but can also occur in a 30 m long version as seen e.g. in a
high-rise pack. In all cases, the hoses are equipped with clutches of the type ’Storch’. The
hoses can be packed in different ways, but it is common to use hose baskets, which can contain
two or three hoses [28][29]. These baskets are specially designed such that the hose can be
deployed while walking with the basket and can be seen in Fig. 2.4. C-hoses can also be
packed as a double-coiled hose for fast deployment of an attack branch, or as a high-rise pack
for fast and space-efficient deployment of an attack branch. Such a pack is illustrated in Fig.
2.4. Lastly, and especially regarding hose tenders, special hose suitcases are used for long hose
connections and can contain between five and seven pre-connected A- or B-hoses. Such hoses
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
15
==PAGE:23==
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.4: (a) A version of the highrise pack and (b) hose baskets
can be deployed from the back of a slow-moving appliance, making long hose-connection faster
to establish.
The Danish FRSs employ many different nozzles but they are typically based on DS/EN 15182,
[14]. Many nozzles allow for individual adjustment of the actual water flow, but in general,
the water flow of the different types of nozzles corresponds to the hose. Thus, generally, a
B-nozzle yields a water flow of 600 L/min while a C-nozzle yields 200 L/min, and so on [18]
These values can be used for conservative estimates.
For personal protection, Danish firefighters wear several layers of personal protective equipment
(PPE). However, it suffices to recognise that during firefighter operations, a firefighter can
generally operate on three levels regarding PPE and firefighter activity [24]:
Standard (Turnout) Uniform: Full firefighter suit, gloves, helmet, and boots - for normal
operations
BA Uniform: The standard uniform, including BA tank, BA mask, ear microphone, and
firefighter hood - for smoke-logged operations
Full Hazardous Incident Suit: BA Uniform, including hazardous suit - for certain hazardous
operations
In general, the BA uniform constitutes an extra load of around 20 kg to the firefighters, while
the hazardous suit is not much heavier but rather, more difficult to maneuver [30]. It should also
be noted, that there is a significant difference between the physical straining during operations
requiring BA uniform without wearing the masks, and operations wearing the masks. Wearing
the mask implies a large dead space in the airways making breathing require more effort [30].
2.1.2.3
Standard Attack Lines
The standard attack lines, defined by DEMA in [31], are a fundamental part of the firefighting
strategies employed by Danish FRSs. These are basic firefighter principles that lay the foundation
for almost all interventions and remain nearly unchanged since they were first defined by Gunnar
Harum in 1978 [22]. In reality, many FRSs deviate to some degree from the standard attacks
e.g. due to a lack of available resources but the procedures still serve as a fundamental baseline.
In each of the standard attack lines, five FFs and one TL are available, including a driver. The
driver is typically responsible for operating the appliance and pump, while the TL is responsible
for the crew and is in charge of the attack line. The remaining four FFs are designated FF
no. 1, FF no. 2, FF no. 3 and FF no. 4, where the designation corresponds to specific tasks.
16
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:24==
Furthermore, it is custom to pair FF no. 1 and no. 2 in SHT 1, and FF no. 3 and no. 4 as
SHT 2.
For future purposes, the following terminology needs to be established: a SOS-bag refers to a
bag containing a nozzle, a pick-up rope, and a hose hook. The bag is typically combined with
a dbl coiled C-hose and is associated with the tasks of FF no. 1. A BB-bag refers to equipment
needed for hydrant operations associated with the tasks of FF no. 4 [31].
The standard attack lines are an HP attack, a B/C attack, a foam attack, a dry-pipe attack,
and lastly an external hauling attack (typically of either HP- or C-hose). Each of these standard
attack lines is presented and summarised in the following sections. However, for this project,
the foam attack is omitted.
The High-Pressure Attack Line
The HP attack is one of the most commonly applied attacks, especially in the early phases of
an intervention, as it is the fastest, easiest, and least taxing attack line. Moreover, due to its
small weight and diameter, an HP hose is ideal for search and rescue operations as it is easy
to maneuver [14][22]. The HP hoses are typically 80m long or at least within the range of
60-120m [31].
The general procedure of establishing an HP attack line, illustrated in Fig. 2.5a, is as follows:
TL - Relays the order, dictates the path of the hose and moves to the set-up area (or
place of an initial attack).
FF no. 1 - Removes the SOS-bag from the appliance, lays out the attack line by the
nozzle to the set-up area, and is assigned to operate the nozzle during the intervention.
FF no. 2 - Lays out the attack line together with FF no. 1 and supports no. 1 in the
advancement of the HP-hose from the set-up area.
FF no. 3 - Assists FF no. 1 and 2 by laying out the attack line and creating adequate
attack coves. When the SHT advances, FF no. 3 relaxes the hose to support a fast
advancement.
FF no. 4 - If needed, FF no. 4 removes the BB-bag and at most two B-baskets to
establish a feeding line from the appliance to a hydrant (or water tender).
An HP attack line can also be established through staircases or by external up-hauling through
windows etc.
The B/C Attack Line
The B/C attack represents the main attack line of Danish FRSs [14]. Although it takes a longer
time and is more taxing to establish than an HP attack, a B/C attack can bring forth a larger
water flow while allowing for multiple attack branches to be established [31] The basic principle
of a B/C attack is to establish a B-line from the appliance with a B/C branch connector at
the end. A branch connector is a fixture which by manual valves, can distribute water from
a B-line to a maximum of three distinct C-lines. The B-line is referred to as an attack line
while the C-lines are referred to as attack branches (numbered from left to right). Thus, a
B/C attack can be established for as long as desired and can supply three C-nozzles for most
firefighting activities.
The general procedure of establishing of a B/C attack line, illustrated in Fig. 2.5b, is as follows:
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
17
==PAGE:25==
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.5: Illustrations of the tasks of FF no. 1, 2, and 3 during (a) an HP
attack line, and (b) a B/C attack line [31]
TL - Relays the order, removes and places the B/C branch connector, dictates the path
of the hose and the set-up area(s), and operates the branch connector.
FF no. 1 - Removes the SOS-bag from the appliance along with a dbl. coiled C-hose.
Moves to the set-up area and lays out the dbl. coiled C-hose perpendicular to the attack
path. Connects one of the ends with the C-nozzle and the other to the C-hose brought
forth by FF no. 2. Furthermore, no. 1 is assigned to operate the nozzle during the
intervention.
FF no. 2 - Removes at most two C-baskets and moves to the branch connector. Lays
out the attack branch from the branch connector, with an adequate number of C-hoses
and attack coves, to the setup area where FF no. 1 is awaiting. Furthermore, no. 2 is
assigned to support no. 1 in the advancement of the attack from the setup area.
FF no. 3 - Removes at most two B-baskets and lays out the attack line from the appliance
to the branch connector. Afterwards, they report to the TL to receive a new assignment,
e.g. operate the branch connector or establish another attack branch, in which case they
mimic the procedure of FF no. 1
FF no. 4 - Removes the BB-bag and at most two B-baskets to establish a feeding
line from the appliance to a hydrant (or water tender). Afterward, they await further
instructions by the appliance to e.g. establish a new attack branch, in which case they
mimic the procedure of FF no. 2.
From the above outlined procedure it is evident, that the maximum number of hoses that can
initially be laid by a single FF is given by two hose baskets i.e. four B- or six C-hoses in total.
Thus, if a longer hose connection is required, the FF needs to return to the appliance to bring
more hose baskets. Just as with the HP attack, a B/C attack can also be established through
staircases or by external up-hauling through windows, etc.
18
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:26==
The Dry-pipe Attack Line
A dry-pipe attack is used in cases where an HP attack cannot reach the fire and a B/C attack
would require unsuitable long and cumbersome hose connections [31]. Naturally, a dry-pipe
attack requires that a dry-pipe system is installed beforehand. It allows for the same water flow
as the B/C attack, and based on an interview with a Danish FRS, a CC branch connector can
be placed on the dry-pipe outlet from which two distinct C-branches can be established. This
is not an official practice and requires a larger outlet pressure from the appliance to ensure
adequate water flow at the end of the nozzles. For redundancy, it is recommended to apply
special dry-pipe equipment on the inlet, from which two distinct B-lines can be established.
Thus, in case of a hose rupture in one of them, there is still sufficient water supply to the
dry-pipe outlet for firefighter protection and activities.
The general procedure of establishing a dry-pipe-attack line, illustrated in Fig. 2.6a, is as
follows:
TL - Relays the order and moves together with FF no. 1 and 2 to the setup area, typically
in a staircase. When FF no. 1 is ready, the TL opens the dry-pipe outlet.
FF no. 1 - Removes the SOS-bag from the appliance along with a dbl. coiled C-hose (or
a high-rise pack). Moves to the setup area and lays out the dbl. coiled C-hose as the
space allows. Connects one of the ends with the C-nozzle and the other to the C-hose
brought forth by FF no. 2. Furthermore, no. 1 is assigned to operate the nozzle during
the intervention.
FF no. 2 - Removes at most two C-baskets (or high-rise packs) and moves to the setup
area. Here, they connect a C-line from the dry-pipe outlet to the C-hose from FF no. 1.
Furthermore, no. 2 is assigned to support no. 1 in the advancement of the attack from
the setup area.
FF no. 3 - Removes a B-basket and the dry-pipe equipment, and lays out the attack
line from the appliance to the dry-pipe inlet and one more from the dry-pipe inlet to the
appliance. Afterward, if no other orders are given, they ensure that all unused dry-pipe
valves are closed and report to the TL to receive a new assignment e.g. establish another
attack branch, in which case they mimic the procedure of FF no. 1
(a)
(b)
Figure 2.6: Illustrations of (a) a dry-pipe attack line, and (b) an exterior B/C
hauling attack [31]
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
19
==PAGE:27==
FF no. 4 - Removes the BB-bag and at most two B-baskets to establish a feeding
line from the appliance to a hydrant (or water tender). Afterward, they await further
instructions by the appliance to e.g. establish a new attack branch in which case they
mimic the procedure of FF no. 2.
It is noted that the dry-pipe system can be pressurised with water already after the first B-line
from the appliance to the dry-pipe inlet. Thus, SHT 1 can begin its advance before FF no. 3
completes the second line.
Exterior Hauling Attack
An exterior hauling attack is a variation to the HP and B/C standard attack lines, where the
attack branch is established on the exterior of a building through an opening e.g. a window
[31]. In general, the procedures are in principle equivalent regardless of it being an HP or a
B/C attack and are only changing the activities of FF no 1 and 2.
In the following, a B/C attack line is used as a baseline. In case of an exterior hauling attack by
an HP attack, the following procedure is adjusted according to the procedure outlined above,
as the overall procedures remain the same.
The general procedure, illustrated in Fig. 2.6b, is as follows:
TL - Relays the order, dictates the path of the hoses, and removes, places, and operates
the B/C branch connector typically close to the up-hauling area.
FF no. 1 - Removes the SOS-bag from the appliance along with a dbl. coiled C-hose.
They ascend to the setup area, where the attack branch is to be hauled, fasten the pick-up
rope, and drop it down to the up-hauling area. When ready, they haul the necessary
amount of C-hoses, fasten the line by a hose hook, and lay out the C-hoses as the space
allows, including the dbl. coiled C-hose. They connect one of the ends with the C-nozzle
and the other to the hauled C-hoses. Furthermore, no. 1 is assigned to operate the
nozzle during the intervention.
FF no. 2 - Removes at most two C-baskets (or an adequate number of dbl. coiled
c-hoses), lays out the attack branch from the branch connector to the up-hauling area,
and fasten the pick-up rope to a C-hose at which point the hauling can commence. They
ensure that the hoses (and in case of an HP attack, the HP nozzle) and the building
facade are not damaged in the process. When an adequate number of hoses have been
hauled, and the connection is established, they ascend to the setup area where FF no. 1
is awaiting. Furthermore, no. 2 is assigned to support no. 1 in the advancement of the
attack from the setup area.
FF no. 3 and 4 - Follow the same procedures as mentioned above.
An exterior hauling can be performed by utilising the C-baskets or by dbl. coiled C-hoses. In
the case of an HP attack, which is generally faster and easier to establish but is limited in
length, subtle changes to the above-mentioned procedure are present. These can be identified
by comparison to the above-mentioned procedure and the procedure of a standard HP attack.
In principle, an exterior hauling attack can also be performed with A- and B-hoses, but this is
rarely the case.
2.1.2.4
Fire Suppression and Extinguishment
There are several ways to suppress and extinguish a fire but the most common is based on
water. Historically, water has been used as the main extinguishing agent for decades and is
20
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:28==
Figure 2.7: Required water application [L/min] (y-axis) versus the Heat Release Rate of the
fire [MW] (x-axis) [14]
often viewed as the direct counterpole to fire. Also, the reliance on water in fire interventions is
built into the design of the emergency services and emergency plans [14][22]. Therefore, water
is often the standard choice and will be the only extinguishing agent considered in this project.
The effect of applying water to a fire is a complex process. There are numerous models which
attempt to describe it to identify the extinguishing capacity of water or more generally, the
needed water application. Fig. 2.7 illustrates several models and their attempts to estimate the
required water application depending on the fire’s current Heat Release Rate.
In [14], the model employed by FBIM [2], is considered a valid and conservative approach. It
concludes that for internal firefighting purposes, every 5 L/s of applied water has an extinguishing
capacity of 8 MW. Similarly, for external firefighting 10 L/s of applied water has an extinguishing
capacity of 5.25 MW, mainly due to less extinguishing effects from steam production [2].
Based on these results, depending on the conditions and the applied water flow, the extinguishing
capacity can be estimated by:

Where VW is the specific applied water flow, QExt.Cap is the extinguish capacity of the given
water flow, and KExt is a conversion factor relating to either internal or external firefighting,
respectively.
Based on the extinguishing capacity, FBIM suggests assessing the effects on the fire, due to
the water application, in three categories based on the fire’s current HRR: decay, constant, and
no effect [2]:
Decay: If QExt.Cap ≥1.1 × ˙Q(twater) - implying that the fire is being extinguished at
some point by the current water application
No effect: If QExt.Cap ≤0.9 × ˙Q(twater) - implying that the water application does not
affect the fire
Constant: If 1.1 × ˙Q(twater) ≤QExt.Cap ≤0.9 × ˙Q(twater) - implying that the fire is
not growing nor decreasing. This could represent a case where the fire is under control,
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design

but further assistance is needed for extinguishing
Utilising these relations, it is possible to assess the extinguishing capacities of the nozzles
employed by the Danish FRSs and impose bonds for the maximal heat release rate of a fire.
This is illustrated in Table 2.1. It is seen that an internal HP-attack line can extinguish a fire
equivalent to a maximal HRR of 2.79 MW, while fire control can occur for a fire at a maximal
HRR of 3.41 MW. Such calculations can be employed to assess the required attack line based
on the heat release rate of a fire. For more details concerning fire suppression, the reader is
referred to [2], [14], [32] and [33].
