2.1.2.5
Strategies for Search Operations
The activity of search and rescue is an important main activity of a general brigade intervention.
The search for occupants is a complex activity that depends on many factors of the fire scene
as well as the skills and physiology of the firefighters. In Denmark, internal search operations
are conducted by firefighters in teams of two as an SHT, equipped with a hose for protection
and fire extinguishing [24]. It is typical to conduct a combination of search and rescue, and
fire extinguishment according to the general principle of ”treat as you go”, i.e. the SHTs will
operate according to what they meet.
In general, when the SHTs are operating in a fire-affected compartment or area, their movements
are restricted to careful walking, creeping, or crawling depending on the conditions concerning
heat and visibility. The best conditions for the firefighters in a fire-affected compartment will
be as close to the floor as possible. Here, it is generally colder and the visibility is better. This
implies less heating of the firefighters and allows for a larger search area, and thus a more
effective search. Therefore, operating close to the floor is challenging and slow, but is safer
and the firefighters can have a longer operation or BA time.
A systematic search operation is a prerequisite for an effective search operation [24]. Therefore,
firefighters are trained in several principles to support a systematic search. The search operation
is conducted with both firefighters in close contact with each other and their hose. A general
(a)
(b)
(c)
Figure 2.8: Search strategies within enclosures of increasing size
22
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:30==
search principle involves following the enclosure of the compartment as illustrated in Fig. 2.8a
The lead firefighter, who operates the nozzle, searches close to the enclosure while the second
firefighter searches as broadly into the room as possible while keeping the contact to the hose.
Thereby, they can cover the perimeter of the enclosure. Depending on the size of the enclosure,
this might not be sufficient to cover the central parts of the enclosure. Thus, it might be
necessary to supplement with diagonal searches or a ”from side to side” search as illustrated in
Fig. 2.8b and 2.8c, until they are certain that the search is completed.
It is common to find occupants near the exits to evacuate or panicked occupants e.g. children in
closets or under beds to hide from the fire. This further illustrates the challenges of performing
a thorough search operation. A thermal camera is a tool that is widely implemented to optimise
the search operation. This tool can help the firefighters to both orientate themselves as well
as to quickly locate occupants in need of rescue. Other factors which influence the search
operations are the furnishing of the room, the size and weight of the hoses, and windows from
which smoke ventilation is possible.
2.2
The Fire Brigade Intervention Model
In this section, a concise presentation of the FBIM methodology is given. It will outline and
present the 16 flowcharts (or charts) and the dataset of 26 tables it relies on [2][3].
The processes of a brigade intervention are often depicted as a series of sequential events
leading to the actual fire extinguishing and rescuing of occupants. One simple model, which
contains the general processes regarding a fire brigade intervention is displayed in Fig. 2.9.
It illustrates a sequential representation of the basic intervention events, divided into several
overall processes and sub-processes. However, such representation mostly serves as a general
qualitative representation and cannot specify the actual intervention activities.
Figure 2.9: Basic sequential representation of a brigade intervention process. FD is an
abbreviation for Fire Department [34].
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
23
==PAGE:31==
A more complex and descriptive model to capture a realistic representation of an intervention
process was developed by the Australian and New Zealand National Council for Fire and
Emergency Services (AFAC). This model is known as the Fire Brigade Intervention Model,
consisting of [2] and [3]. FBIM is capable of describing the intervention process to a very high
degree of detail while remaining general enough to be applied to a large variety of fire scenarios.
Furthermore, the model is associated with temporal values, based on an extensive investigation,
which enables a quantitative representation for most of the individual steps of the intervention
process.
FBIM is in this project, subjected to further investigation from which its application in a Danish
context is sought to be evaluated and validated. Previous Danish investigations have illustrated
the use of this model revealing a potential to describe Danish intervention activities [35][36].
Thus, allowing a qualitative analysis to e.g. evaluate a building design concerning its capacity
to support an effective intervention, evaluate and compare different intervention strategies, and
reveal critical and bottle-neck activities.
2.2.1
Application of FBIM Flowcharts
The 16 flowcharts of FBIM, provided in the manual [2], lays the foundation for this project
as each chart serves to describe specific activities of a fire intervention, as illustrated in the
overview in Fig. 2.10. A generalised intervention process can be separated into four phases:
the pre-intervention phase, the investigation and initiation phase, the main intervention phase,
and the end of intervention phase, as illustrated in Fig. 2.11. Each FBIM chart in [2] consists
of sequences of numbered ’event-boxes’, which either refer to a decision point (depicted as a
rhombus), or to an activity from which a time duration is to be added by referring to sub-charts
or directly to data tables in [3].
The first three phases of an FBIM simulation are generally the most well-defined. These allow
to estimate the arrival times, the time for established water connections, and the time when
the firefighters are ready to engage the fire or perform search and rescue. Depending on the
scenario, the end of the intervention phase is still out of reach for FBIM, as indicated in [35].
For a simple fire scenario, where the heat release rate of the fire can be properly estimated,
the end of intervention involves maintaining the attack line and completing the search and
rescue, while substituting the firefighters when needed. However, no real fire scene is simple
and everything depends on the specific fire development in a specific setting. For this reason,
this project is mainly focused on the first three phases; the core of FBIM.
Each phase of a general intervention, as illustrated in Fig. 2.11, can be summarised as follows:
The Pre-Intervention Phase - This phase describes and quantifies all the processes from
when the fire is detected to the response is on the kerbside of the fire scene. It involves
the sequential application of the FBIM Charts 1, 2, 3, and 4, for each appliance in the
first call-out. For secondary responses, the sequence begins with Chart 2. The phase is
mainly dependent on the design and system parameters of the fire scene and the deployed
response. It can thereby be generalised to a whole building, building section, or cadastral
parcel if the detection systems are the same. It should be supported by fire dynamical
considerations, indicating the activation times of the specific systems.
The Investigation and Initiation Phase - This phase describes and quantifies the operations
of the OIC (officer in charge). They need to assess the situation, determine the fire
location, and extend before the individual FFs are initiated. This involves the sequential
application of the FBIM Charts 5 and 7, including the sub-Charts 6 and 9. Furthermore,
the phase is also able to describe the request and dispatch of additional resources, should
24
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:32==
the first call-out not be assessed as sufficient. This opens up new trajectories in the
FBIM simulation, beginning with Chart 2. The FBIM manual does not provide the
means to describe the activity of the OIC to relay orders and the FFs to receive them.
In [35], it is assumed equivalent to the parameter C1: ’time to receive and take down
verbal information’, offered in the dataset, as a suggested solution. The phase is mainly
dependent on the parameters of the fire scene and fire case, and can to some extent
be generalised to a whole building section. It should be supported by evacuation and
fire dynamical considerations to follow the development on the scene as required when
choosing the different paths in the FBIM charts.
Figure 2.10: Overview of FBIM Methodology and Flowcharts [2]
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
25
==PAGE:33==
Figure 2.11: Overview of the ’Chain of Events’ of a generalised intervention process,
with two distinct fire brigade responses, each associated with two SHTs, analogous to
those applied in [35]
The Main Intervention Phase - This phase describes and quantifies the initial and
secondary intervention activities. It describes and accounts for the activities of each
Smoke-Helmet Team (SHT) performing their main tasks of establishing attack and
feeding lines, performing search and rescue, initiating fire control, and if possible, fire
26
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:34==
extinguishing, as well as ensuring or minimising property damage and fire spread. The
phase involves the application of FBIM Charts 8, 10, 12, 13, and 15, including the
sub-Charts 6, 9, 11 and 14. The order of application is a question of fire scenario,
strategy, priorities, iterations, and experience, but it can be beneficial to separate into
several trajectories for each of the SHTs (and sometimes even for each FF).
The End of Intervention Phase - This phase describes and quantifies the last stages
of the intervention activities.
Depending on the fire scene, this phase consists of
ensuring the completion of the activities initiated in the main intervention phase, while
a long-term intervention strategy is deployed which finalises the intervention. Thus,
this phase describes and accounts for the substitution and rest breaks of the SHTs for
continuous operation and the deployment of additional resources necessary to complete
the intervention including environmental considerations. Generally, this phase involves
the continued application of FBIM Charts 12, 13, and 15, while initiating Chart 16 along
with other special activities such as substitution, clean-up, and damage assessment and
control. This is typically done in cooperation with insurance companies and damage
services together with the owners and can take several days to perform, depending on
the fire scenario.
The phases described above are a general categorisation of a full brigade intervention, from fire
detection to the final transfer of the fire scene to other authorities by the OIC. Each phase is
associated with specific FBIM charts and needs to be tailored to the specific case in question.
Each phase along with the application of the FBIM charts will later be analysed and discussed
in depth, in relation to the Danish practices described in Section 2.1.2.
2.2.2
The FBIM Dataset
The datasets that FBIM relies on are presented in this section. The input data can be categorised
by intervention activities, each representing a key feature of a generalised standard intervention:
Notification Parameters: A, B, D
Departure Parameters: E, F1
Communication Parameters: C, H, L
Entry Parameters: G, I, J, K, PX2, S
Safety Parameters: N, O2, O3, O4

Water Set Up Parameters: O1, P1, P2, V, W, X
Above, the tables from the FBIM dataset [3] are referred to by their designated letter. Specific
elements, written in bold, are further specified with a number.
Below is a summary of the activities and available input parameters in the current FBIM dataset.
In cases where the tables do not supply a value, three terms are applied to distinguish between
where the information is supposed to be gathered: D2S (Designer to Supply), TBA (To be
Advised), and U2S (User to Supply). Thus, D2S means that the designers and manufacturers
need to supply the given information, whereas U2S means that it is the user of FBIM that
somehow needs to supply the information. Lastly, TBA indicates that the information should
be based on advice from FRS authorities.

The input data outlined above is based on an extensive investigation performed by AFAC,
including the execution of several firefighting drills. A description of these firefighting drills is
available in the Appendix E, and will be used as a guideline for the drills in this project.
Although not directly related to use in an FBIM context, a similar approach has been employed
in [14], from which a series of datasets have been made available. Of particular interest are
the findings of ’Time for Brigade Notification’ (chapter 4.2), ’Time for Departure’ (chapter
4.3), and ’Travel Times’ (chapter 4.5). These could be directly implemented as a part of
30
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:38==
Charts 1 to 4 in Danish FBIM simulations. Moreover, based on a series of firefighting drills of
several variations of the standard attack lines, the time of completion for these is also available,
based on [22]. However, these drills and the results thereof, are too case-specific to be used
in a general FBIM setting but will serve as guidelines for the drills in this project. Other
investigations regarding firefighter horisontal and vertical travel speeds is available in [37] and
[36], which suggests analogous results to those provided by the FBIM Dataset [3].
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
31
==PAGE:39==
Chapter 3
Method
This chapter is devoted to presenting the methodology of this project. The aim is to present
how the results are obtained and how they need to be perceived. Based on the theory provided
in the previous chapter, Section 3.1 serves to identify and determine the experimental scope
employed in this project. This is achieved by a review and assessment of the FBIM dataset,
illuminating the parts of FBIM that cannot be directly assumed applicable in a Danish setting.
By this assessment and in close cooperation with the firefighters of Copenhagen Airport Rescue
and Firefighting (ARFF), four experiments are devised as presented in Section 3.2. Section 3.3
serves to describe how the measurements were performed, illustrate the challenges, and estimate
the associated errors. Furthermore, it serves to present the methodology of the statistical
analysis, applied in this project. Section 3.4 and 3.5 are devoted to the development of a
digital representation of FBIM, and how this software is utilised in the FBIM simulations of
this project, respectively.
3.1
Experimental Scope
To achieve the objectives of this project, a series of experiments needs to be devised. This
section will present and describe the experimental scope, along with the arguments for the
given selection. For simplicity, this project is limited to standard fire interventions, i.e. incidents
involving hazardous materials or special rescue (DA: udfrielse/befrielse) are outside the scope.
The only extinguishing agent considered is water (assumed analog to simple foam-/CAF-
systems). Uncommon water supply lines, such as serial pumping (DA: seriepumpning), pumping
from a static reservoir, or the charging of dry-pipe systems, are not considered.
Due to time limitations, it is not possible to investigate all necessary parts of the FBIM.
Therefore, the experimental scope aims to identify and outline the activities to be included in
the preliminary investigation of this project.
3.1.1
Review and Assessment of FBIM Data
In this section, all the data required for FBIM simulations is reviewed and discussed to prioritise
the data and thus, to define the scope of the experiments. The aim is to identify where to
direct the focus of this project and to get a better understanding of the parameters which
FBIM relies on.
To demonstrate where each parameter is applied, each intervention phase is further outlined
and discussed from the perspectives of a general standard intervention as illustrated in Fig.
32
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:40==
2.11. The review is based on [35][36], and the assessment is performed from the perspectives
of Danish firefighting procedures and intervention strategies.
3.1.1.1
The Pre-Intervention Phase
The first part of the pre-intervention phase, Charts 1 and 2, is mainly dependent on design
parameters of the technical detection-, suppression-, and dispatch systems (Tables A, B, and D).
The data that these charts rely on, requires technical validations, which will not be investigated
further in this thesis. Table C is also applied if the fire notification is verbal and represents the
time to receive and take down the information. It can be argued that this process is similar no
matter the geographic location and thereby, the given value is assumed applicable.
Chart 3 is mainly dependent on how the FRS is organised, reflected in the values available in
Table E. For the activities of ’dress, assimilate information and depart’ (E2), and ’make up
and become mobile’ (E3), no significant difference between Danish firefighters and others is
expected. Therefore, these data are considered applicable. However, for the activity of ’travel
to fire station, (...) and leave station’ (E1), which reflects a part-time FRS organisation, a
significant discrepancy might be present. In Denmark, part-time FRSs have a five-minute rule
for the activity of E1. It can therefore be argued that eight minutes for this activity, might be
too conservative. However, such investigation lies outside the scope of this project.
The last activity in the pre-intervention phase is the traveling from the fire station to the fire
scene, described in Chart 4. For this chart, the FBIM manual relies on the use of Google
Maps™to estimate the given travel times, represented by Table F. This is considered a very
conservative approach, as it does not take into account that the fire brigades are allowed to
overrule the normal traffic laws when responding to an emergency. However, FRS travel time is
not investigated further in this project, and the reader is referred to [14], for data concerning
traveling times.
3.1.1.2
The Investigation and Initiation Phase
The first part of the investigation and initiation phase, i.e. Chart 5, relies on data for on-site
travel speeds, building entries (forced or with keys), communication with fire wardens, way-
finding and information gathering from an FDCIE1. Moreover, Chart 5 relies on sub-Charts
6 and 9, which describe the dismount and the donning of safety equipment, and firefighter
travel times, respectively. Thus, this chart relies on data from numerous tables from the FBIM
dataset but only a few are expected to be significantly different in a Danish setting.
Gaining access to a building, either forced or by keys (Tables I, J, and P), is an activity that is
expected to be similar in execution regardless of geographic location. The same can be argued
to be true for communication with fire wardens (Table H) and way-finding (Table K). For the
information gathering from an FDCIE (Table L), the FDCIE could be quite different, but it is
expected that the essence is the same. These data are therefore assessed to be applicable and
will not be investigated further in this project. However, it should be noted that the activity
of forced entry, might be highly dependent on the tool set available by the FRS. The on-site
travel speed (F2) is also considered applicable, but conservative, especially if the on-site travel
distance is large. As mentioned, this will not be investigated further in this project. Lastly,
Chart 5 also relies on inputs for a hindrance factor, due to any evacuees (Table S) and security
procedures (Table G). These are undefined and require the user to supply, and will therefore
not be investigated any further as they depend on the given evacuation model, and on the
given type of security procedures, respectively.
1Fire Detection Control and Indicating Equipment, assumed equivalent with the Danish term: ABA-system.
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
33
==PAGE:41==
In sub-Chart 6, the dismount of the appliance can only happen in conjunction with the donning
of BA (Table M). Thereby, it is not possible to simulate an OIC or FF to dismount the appliance
without donning BA, which might be the case when the fire central is out of harm’s way. It
also creates an inconsistency if applying Table N in Chart 6, as the sub-activity: ’don BA’ is
performed twice. This issue and adjustment of Table M or N lies outside the scope of this
project and must be dealt with at a later time. Sub-Chart 6 also relies on data regarding
hazardous incidents (from Tables N and O), and such incidents will not be investigated further.
Sub-Chart 6 also entails the activity of flushing hydrants (O1) and the removal of standard
equipment/tools from the appliance (Table P). Due to the difference in equipment and standard
procedures, these are selected to be investigated further and extended to reflect the Danish
standard fire attack lines, presented in the theory.
Sub-Chart 9, which describes the firefighter travel times, depends on doors to be negotiated
(Tables I and J) as in Chart 5, horisontal travel speeds (Table Q), along with vertical travel
speeds, either in a firefighter elevator (Table R) or in a staircase (Table T). Due to the expected
difference in equipment and the fact that firefighter travel speeds play a significant role in FBIM
simulation, Tables Q and T are sought to be validated by Danish data values. Furthermore, it is
noted, that in FBIM, there is currently no clear distinction between FFs in BA uniforms wearing
the face mask and FFs in BA uniforms not wearing the face mask. It is recommended that this
is implemented at some point in its future development. However, such an investigation will
not be a part of this project.
The next part of the investigation and initiation phase is the initiation of the firefighters, i.e.
the activity of the OIC to relay orders and the firefighters to receive them. This is not covered
by the FBIM manual but is implemented in [35], by assuming C1 to be applicable. In a Danish
context, OICs are trained in the concept of a five-point-order (DA: fempunkts befaling) [17],
and the 60 seconds from C1 is considered appropriate, but is highly dependent on the complexity
of the fire scene. Investigation of the activity of ’relay and receive orders’, is not the main
objective of this project, but as it plays a key role in any intervention process, it cannot be
dismissed.
The last part of the investigation and initiation phase consists of Chart 7, which again depends on
firefighter travel speeds (Tables Q, R, and T), which have already been discussed. Furthermore,
Chart 7 enables a new sequence of events by applying Chart 2, when requesting for additional
resources.
3.1.1.3
The Main Intervention Phase
The main intervention phase is initiated by the orders from the OIC. It begins with the use
of Chart 8, which relies on data that have already been outlined and discussed above. For
transparency and to ensure appropriate use of FBIM, it is at this point advised to separate into
Smoke-Helmet Teams, or even into each firefighter. By Chart 8, each SHT has traveled to their
designated set-up area with the required equipment and PPE (personal protective equipment),
depending on their given tasks. The next part of a generalised intervention is to establish
attack lines and feeding lines, described by Charts 10 and 11.
Chart 10 is mainly dependent on the positioning of the fire appliance (Table U), the horisontal
and vertical travel speeds (Tables Q and T), as well as the laying, connecting, and charging of
hoses (Table V). Furthermore, Chart 10 also relies on sub-Charts 11 and 9. The data concerning
Chart 9 and firefighter travel speeds has been discussed above. If a water tanker is applied,
Chart 10 may describe this by the loop from Box 6. Depending on the water consumption it
might still be necessary to connect to hydrants or similar to ensure long-term supply. However,
34
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:42==
this might be de-prioritised at the beginning of the intervention while the primary tasks are
carried out.
The positioning of the fire appliance is considered applicable, as it is expected to be rather
slow and cautious, and applicable for very small distances. However, the activity of laying,
connecting, and charging of hoses, requires a modification to be applicable in a Danish setting.
The main reason is the fact that the diameters of the hoses in Table V do not correspond to the
hoses applied by the Danish FRSs. Furthermore, there seems to be some inconsistency in the
data available in Table V, as some include the removal of the hoses from the appliance (V1-V3),
while the remaining does not. This mismatch implied some user challenges as indicated in [35].
To achieve a higher degree of transparency which will make it easier to use, some of the data
from Table V will be further investigated to be aligned with the Danish standards of fire attack
lines and feeding lines, presented in theory. Furthermore, it should be noted that the time delay
due to the charging of a dry-pipe system is not included in FBIM simulations and that there
are currently no data values regarding the activity of laying and charging HP hoses nor hoses
from a high-rise pack.
Chart 11 mainly describes the process of establishing feeding lines to ensure adequate water
supply for long-term firefighting. Besides the laying, connecting, and charging of hoses (Table
V), discussed above, this process is dependent on the activity of locating a hydrant (Table
W) and on the activity of obtaining water from a static water source, e.g. a lake or reservoir
(Table X). No significant discrepancy in the activity of obtaining water from a static water
source is expected and it is seldom necessary in Denmark. Thus, the values of Table X will not
be investigated further in this project. The same applies to the activity of locating a hydrant.
However, it should be noted that in Denmark, all fire hydrants are marked within the so-called
GIS (Geographic Information System), implying that firefighters can easily and quickly locate
the nearest fire hydrants [27]. Therefore, it can be argued that the data provided in Table W,
are not applicable in a Danish setting and could be described with a single value. However,
such a value will not be investigated in this project as it is considered a minor part of the
intervention. As in Chart 7, Chart 11 also enables requesting additional resources by Charts
2-4, which in this context could be a water tanker or resources for a long feeding line.
The last part of the main intervention phase is the concurrent execution of the activities: fire
control and extinguishing, search and rescue, and exposure protection, described by Charts 15,
12, and 13, respectively. The order in which these are initiated depends on the fire scene and
the assessment by the OIC. At the beginning of the main intervention, a quick suppression
might be beneficial in some cases while insignificant in others. For simplicity it is assumed that
the FRS’s initial priorities are twofold: to rescue and to protect. If both of these can be fulfilled
simultaneously by a quick suppression followed by search and rescue, and exposure protection,
an offensive tactic can be employed [17][24]. If the fire size exceeds the initial capacity of the
FRS, a defensive tactic might be more appropriate, letting search and rescue, and exposure
protection be a higher priority. Therefore, the sequential application of Charts 15, 12, and 13 is
dictated by the FBIM user and the given fire scenario.
Chart 15 relies on sub-Chart 11, but is mainly dependent on the user input regarding the heat
release rate of the fire at the time of water application, and the volumetric flow rate of the
attack(s). When the heat release rate of the fire can be properly estimated, Chart 15 allows to
evaluate the effects of the water application in three categories: decay, constant, and no effect.
The decaying effect is in this context equivalent to fire extinguishing, while the constant effect
is equivalent to fire control. This can be an important distinction in the assessment of resource
allocation, as the extinguishing of a controlled fire can be postponed while search and rescue is
completed. In cases where the heat release rate cannot be estimated, Chart 15 still allows to
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
35
==PAGE:43==
estimate the time of initiated attacks and qualitatively evaluate the control and extinguishing
process, as illustrated in [35].
Chart 12 describes the activities of search and rescue. It requires the user to provide the number
of search teams, the required search areas, and search tactics (primary or secondary), while
reminding the user to evaluate the fire development and the firefighter safety level. It depends
on the data from Tables Q, R, S, T, and Y to simulate the search, as well as sub-Chart 9
for further reconnaissance. The data from Tables Q, R, S, and T, is used to simulate the
firefighters’ ”normal” travel to the designated area, where the search and rescue is to be
performed. These Tables along with sub-Chart 9, have been discussed above. The actual search
and rescue is simulated by the data from Table Y, containing data regarding primary search
(Y3/Q3), secondary search (Y1), and the rescue of a person (Y2). Table Y also reminds the
user that a prolonged search and rescue operation requires staged BA control and replenishing
of BA and recovery. However, no data for these activities is given.
The application of Chart 12 has several issues as indicated in [35]. The first issue is regarding
the need to define the number of search teams, and then only use half the number to perform
the search. This is due to the inbuilt feature of crew rotation within the FBIM, which is indeed
very important. However, as suggested in [35] it might be more prudent to apply the FBIM
charts for the individual SHTs, letting the user in charge of the book-keeping. This could
provide more transparency and allow for more realistic simulations and comparisons with logged
events. However, it could also introduce inconsistencies in use, as it will depend on the user’s
ability to map out and log the activities while adjusting when needed. Moreover, Chart 12
does not allow for simulating rescue activities using portable ladders or appliance ladders. Nor
does it take into account that search and rescue is often aided by thermal cameras or can be
obstructed by objects implying a prolonged freeing process. These challenges lie outside the
scope of this project and must be dealt with at a later time. However, as search and rescue
operations are one of the most important and time-consuming intervention activities, the simple
cases of Table Y sought to be investigated and extended to include search and rescue aided by
thermal cameras.
Depending on the specific fire scenario, Chart 13, describing exposure protection, i.e. the
prevention of fire spread between properties or fire compartments, can be applied in either
the main intervention phase or at the end of intervention phase. If the fire is large compared
with the resources available for the FRS, it can be justified to perform the exposure control
before the control and extinguishing of the fire. This is a defensive strategy, often referred
to as a controlled burn, as the main fire is allowed to burn but is prevented from spreading
any further. In cases where the fire is smaller, the exposure control can be de-prioritised and
performed later in the intervention process. Thus, as mentioned, the chronological application
of Chart 15 or Chart 13 is highly dependent on the fire size and the resources available, as it is a
matter of strategic assessment. Nonetheless, Chart 13 is placed in the main intervention phase.
It relies on the sub-Charts 14, 9, and 11, and allows for requesting additional resources by
Charts 2-11. Furthermore, Chart 13 enables the use of aerial equipment for exposure protection
(Table Z). The data provided in Table Z is considered outside the scope of this project but
is considered appropriate. Thereby, all input values required in Chart 13 have been outlined,
except for sub-Chart 14 which is applied in special situations where fire spread is not the only
concern of the FRS. In such cases, Chart 14 describes a salvage operation or a process to
protect un-involved contents. It relies on sub-Charts 6 and 92 but requires the user to estimate
the time of the main activities and thus, remain a qualitative tool.
2The FBIM manual states Charts 6 and 7, but this is considered an error
36
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:44==
3.1.1.4
The End of Intervention Phase
The end of intervention phase consists of maintaining and concluding the activities of the SHTs,
by ensuring substitution for continuous operation, while implementing a long-term intervention
strategy. For substitution, 20-30 minutes of BA operation is advised as a guideline, but will
naturally vary depending on the specific tasks. The long-term intervention strategy, if needed,
generally contains the activities of supporting the conclusion of Charts 15, 12, and 13, and
performing Chart 16.
Chart 16 describes a process of environmental protection from water runoff, but is essentially a
tool to prioritise between fighting the fire (enabled by Chart 11) or preventing the spill (enabled
by Charts 2-8). Chart 16, remains a qualitative tool, as it merely opens up for additional
mobilisation of resources while requiring the user to define the time of determining the burning
materials and their environmental impact. Evidently, simulations of the end of intervention
phase do not provide many insightful results. However, it still has much merit as it provides a
framework for the user to understand and plan for a holistic fire intervention.
3.1.2
Applicability of FBIM Parameters
In Table 3.1, the data is summarised and rated for its direct applicability in a Danish context.
For each data value, the charts that depend on it are indicated along with the given temporal
value. If the value is described by a statistical distribution, it is indicated by a ”∼”-symbol and
the mean is provided.
In the table, a rating system of the applicability of the data in Danish FBIM simulations is
shown. The system is based on the reasoning of the activities they represent, and arguments
for why they should be different in a Danish setting, as described in Section 3.1.1. The ranking
system aims to distinguish between activities assessed to be well represented by the data
(
), somewhat or questionable represented (∼), and not possible/able to be evaluated (-).
The activities deemed well represented by data, are activities that cannot be argued to be
significantly different, and can therefore be assumed to be represented by the result of the
substantial research behind the FBIM data. The activities ranked as somewhat or questionable
represented by the FBIM data, are activities where significant discrepancy might be present
due to differences in equipment, procedure, or likewise. The activities not possible to evaluate
indicate areas outside the scope of this project.
3.1.3
Scope of Experiments
In the review and assessment of the FBIM data provided above, several activities with ques-
tionable applicability in Danish FBIM simulations have been identified. Among these, only
a few have been selected to be included in this project due to time limitations. These have
been selected based on their role within a general intervention process, and whether they are
represented in the current FBIM format. The selected activities are summarised in Table 3.2,
where it is indicated whether the activity is sought to be validated or implemented. Furthermore,
it is indicated in which experiment the selected activities will be contained within.
As evident by the selection in Table 3.2, the scope of this project has been directed towards
implementing the Danish standard attack lines and the basic principles of Danish firefighting
procedures. This is evident by the major suggested extensions to Tables P and V, along with the
associated activity from Table O. Due to its critical role during an intervention, the activities of
search and rescue in Table Y, have also been included in this project, along with a suggestion
of extension by including the option of being aided by thermal cameras. Lastly, horisontal and

stair travel speeds have also been included in this project, as they also represent the main

The author was granted the honor to cooperate with the fire and rescue services of Copenhagen
Airport (ARFF: Airport Rescue and Firefighting) and their associated academy CARFA (Copen-
hagen Airport Rescue and Firefighting Academy). CARFA granted the permission to utilise their
training facilities, seen in Fig. 3.1, while ARFF agreed to execute a series of firefighting drills to
be documented and analysed. In close cooperation with these, a series of four experiments were
devised. The first three experiments aim to validate or gather values of individual activities
while the last experiment aims for an overall validation of the model by a fire scenario to be
compared with FBIM simulations.
3.2
Experimental Setup
In this project, a series of four main experiments have been devised. This section aims to
provide a brief description of the framework of each experiment to provide the relevant and
necessary context to the final results obtained in the project. The experiments were defined in
cooperation with CARFA and ARFF and reflect the available facilities and resources.
3.2.1
Experiment 1 - The Physical Test
The author was granted the honor of attending some of the firefighters’ physical tests and
measuring certain firefighting activities during this test. It is recognised that measuring during
a physical test is not optimal for gathering ’clean’ data, as the measurements might be affected
by this fact. However, in an effort to collect as much representative data as possible, these
measurements are included. It is considered that the measurements gathered during the physical
test might reflect the fatigue that firefighters will experience during an intervention. The
activities sought to be measured in this experiment, are given in Table 3.2.
Experiment Description
The physical tests were performed in full turnout gear including BA equipment. It consisted of
the following activities, presented in a chronological sequence, depending on the age of the
firefighters
1. 300m walk w/o equipment
2. 100m walk w/ equipment (2 C-baskets)
3. Hoist of three B-/C-hoses
4. 35/30 hits with a sledgehammer
5. 35/25 knee-bends with a 20kg kettlebell
6. 40/30 steps on a 20cm high step bench
7. 25/20 lifts from the pelvis to the thorax with a 15kg barbell
8. 40m backward drag with a 70kg iron chain
Thus, the activities that were measured were no. 1, 2, and 8, including the ascend and descend
stairs in connection with the third activity. All measurements were conducted with stop-watches
and the results were rounded off to the nearest second. The distances were not exact, especially
for the first activity, but the results are still considered applicable. The documented results of
the experiment are provided in Appendix B.
40
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:48==
3.2.2
Experiment 2 - The Danish Standard Attack Lines
In Denmark, there are currently five standard fire attacks. As mentioned, these include high-
pressure attacks, B/C attacks, foam attacks, dry-pipe attacks, and an external hauling attack.
They are described in greater detail in Section 2.1.2. One of the main objectives of this project
is to incorporate these standard attacks into FBIM. Therefore, in this experiment, some attack
lines are performed and measured during simple training drills, i.e. not during a fire scenario.
The activities sought to be measured in this experiment, are given in Table 3.2. This section will
only provide a brief description of the experiment, and the reader is referred to Appendix A, for
a complete description, and an overview of the necessary equipment. Similar, the documented
results of the experiment is are provided in Appendix B.
Experiment Description
For this experiment, four simple training drills are devised to include the activities mentioned
above and to reflect the most common standard attack lines. The drills included in this part
are an HP attack, a B/C attack, a dry-pipe attack, and an exterior hauling attack. The
starting point for each drill is identical where all of the involved firefighters are gathered near
the appliance, dressed in full turnout gear. The appliance, in idle mode, has all its cabinets
closed off, and when ready, the Team Leader initiates the drill loud and clear. The endpoint for
each drill is also identical and marked by the discharge of water on the 2. floor of the training
building, signaling that SHT 1, is ready to advance and initiate the firefighting operations.
All involved firefighters are beforehand briefed in detail about the content and execution of
each drill. This is done to ensure that all the necessary details are captured and to ensure the
validity of the results. Since this experiment is exclusively focused on documenting individual
sub-activities, the results are considered unaffected by this fact.
Figure 3.2: Sketched overview of the experimental set-up of experiment 2
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
41
==PAGE:49==
The times of each activity are recorded by a series of stationary cameras, and all the drills
are to be carried out at ’normal fire ground speed’. When each drill is complete all relevant
distances are measured and documented. The experimental set-up is seen in Fig. 3.2
Experiment 2.a - The HP Attack
All firefighters are dressed in full turnout gear with BA but no masks, and gathered near the
appliance. FF no. 4 is to establish a feeding line with a B-basket from the appliance to a fire
hydrant and walk back to the appliance. SHT 1 shall set up the attack hose, discharge water
to adjust the nozzle, advance a number of stairs to the 2. floor, and discharge water to mark
the completion of the drill.
Firefighter no. 3 is to support the roll-out of the HP hose, while the driver is to support the
appliance connections and operate the pump to charge the hose when ready.
A sketch of the drill is shown in Fig. 3.3. Relevant distances to be noted are three lengths of
B-hose for the feeding line (45m) and the full extent of the HP-hose (90m).
Experiment 2.b - The B/C Attack
All firefighters are dressed in full turnout gear with BA but no masks, and gathered near the
appliance. FF no. 4 is to establish a feeding line with two B-baskets from the appliance to a
fire hydrant and walk back to the appliance. FF no. 3 is to establish the main attack line with
two B-baskets and walk back to the appliance. FF no. 2 is to establish the attack branch with
two C-baskets from the branch connector to the set-up area.
Firefighter no. 1 is to walk to the set-up area, lay out a double-coiled C-hose, and connect it
to the nozzle and to the hose brought by firefighter no. 2. Afterwards, no. 1 is to call for the
branch to be charged and discharge water to adjust the nozzle7. Then, SHT 1 is to advance a
number of stairs to the 2. floor and discharge water to mark the completion of the drill. The
driver is to support the appliance connections and operate the pump to charge the line when
ready, while the OIC is to place and operate the branch connector.
7Note that V2.2 DK and V4.2 DK are carried out by both firefighters no. 1 and 2.

Experiment 2.c - The Dry-pipe Attack
All firefighters are dressed in full turnout gear with BA but no masks, and gathered near the
appliance. FF no. 4 is to establish a feeding line with two B-baskets from the appliance to a
fire hydrant and walk back to the appliance. Firefighter no. 3 is to establish the connection
from the appliance to the dry-pipe system with a B-basket and the dry-pipe equipment. For
safety redundancy, a parallel connection is laid subsequently from the dry-pipe to the appliance.
SHT 1 is to establish the attack-branch from the dry-pipe outlet by the use of a high-rise pack.
After connecting and charging the high-rise pack, including the discharge of water to adjust
the nozzle, SHT 1 is to advance a number of stairs to the 2. floor and discharge water to mark
the completion of the drill. The driver is to support the appliance connections and operate the
pump to charge the line when ready.
A sketch of the drill is shown in Fig. 3.5. Relevant distances to be noted are three lengths of
B-hose (45m) for the feeding line, one length of B-hose (15m) for the main attack line (from
appliance to dry-pipe inlet), one length of C-hose of a high-rise pack (30 m) for the attack
branch.
Experiment 2.d - The Exterior Hauling Attack
All firefighters are dressed in full turnout gear with BA but no masks, and gathered near the
appliance. FF no. 4 is to establish a feeding line with two B-baskets from the appliance to a
fire hydrant and walk back to the appliance. Firefighter no. 3 is to establish the main attack
line with two B-baskets, from appliance to branch connector.
Firefighter no. 1 is to ascend to the set-up area, where the attack branch is to be hauled. Then
they fasten the pick-up rope and drop it down to firefighter no. 2 awaiting by the hauling area
below. Firefighter no. 2 is to establish the attack-branch from branch-connector to the hauling
area with two C-baskets and guide the hauling from the ground. The hauling is done from
the C-baskets, by fastening the pick-up rope dropped from above. They now call no. 1 to
Figure 3.6: Sketch of experiment 2d, the exterior hauling attack line. Green zone
indicates set-up area, and turquoise indicates the end zone

start hoisting the hose, while controlling the hose from the ground. When an adequate number
of hoses have been hauled, and the connection is established, they ascend to the set-up area
where FF no. 1 awaits. When ready, SHT 1 calls for the branch to be charged and discharge
water to mark the completion of the drill.
A sketch of the drill is shown in Fig. 3.4. Relevant distances to be noted are three lengths of

3.2.3
Experiment 3 - Search & Rescue Operations
For this experiment, two identical drills are devised to include and reflect both a search operation
and a rescue operation. They are described in greater detail in Section 2.1.2. As mentioned it
is desired to validate the current FBIM data in a Danish context regarding secondary search
(Y1) and the rescuing of a person (Y2), as well as to incorporate the option for a secondary
search aided with thermal camera (Y1.2 DK), see Table 3.2. This section will only provide a
brief description of the experiment and the reader is referred to Appendix A, for a complete
description. The documented results of the experiments are provided Appendix B.
A search operation is a very complex process to represent by a single parameter. It is highly
dependent on the topology and furnishing of the compartments as well as the skills and
physiology of the given firefighters. A search operation depends on the heat- and smoke
development within each compartment, and the options for smoke ventilation. The heat and
visibility define how the firefighters can move around and how big an area they can cover.
Thus, to represent all these different factors with a single applicable estimate for the search
speeds implies the need to simplify the process, and to conduct and document a large variety of
search operations. In this project, only one search operation is conducted with a small variety
of envelope topology and search conditions.
Experiment Description
The two drills are carried out by two different SHTs, who are prevented from talking to each
other in between to avoid prior knowledge of the scenario. In the first drill, the SHT is to
perform the search operation by traditional means while the second drill is performed with the
aid of a thermal camera. To simulate a real search and rescue operation, the search premises are
darkened and smoke-logged, and each SHT is equipped with an HP hose, which is supported at
the entry point. The rescue operation is simulated by a training dummy of 70kg to simulate an
average person. The search team is not allowed to ventilate the premises. The starting point
for each drill is identical as begins when the SHT advances through the entry point. Similarly,
the endpoint is given when the search is complete, and the training dummy has been rescued.
Inspired by the procedures of the corresponding FBIM drill, Appendix E, the search operation is
sought to be represented by an area speed, i.e. m2/s. Ideally, this would require the introduction
of a grid on the flooring and cameras in the ceiling to accurately estimate the search speeds of
each grid. However, as this was not possible due to the need for a darkened and smoke-logged
environment, making the grid impossible to observe, another approach was adopted.
The search premises were separated into several zones utilising the design of the premises. Each
SHT was then accompanied by an observer, recording the process through a thermal camera.
The zones could then be identified by certain markers that were not directly apparent but
were visible when knowing what to look for. Furthermore, each zone corresponds to different
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
45
==PAGE:53==
Figure 3.7: Sketch of experiment 3, indicating each search zone and the
corresponding dimensions
topologies, posing different challenges. Thereby, the obtained results can be considered to
represent some variety of search operations. The premises, including each search zone, can be
seen in Fig. 3.7. It is seen that all the rooms are mostly empty.
Each SHT was specifically instructed to search left around the enclosure, to ensure that the
desired search pattern was followed. In Fig. 3.7, the search direction can be seen, and it is
assumed that an SHT can cover a width of around 2.4m perpendicular to the search direction,
which is considered valid. Moreover, the location of the training dummy can also be seen along
with the furnishing of each room.
As seen in the sketch, zone 1 represents an empty corridor, zone 2 represents an empty part of
a larger room, zone 3 represents a more complex topology with few objects, zone 4 represents
a part of a larger room with an obstacle in the form of a staircase, and zone 5 represents a part
of a larger room with few objects, including the training dummy. When found, the training
dummy is to be rescued immediately, interrupting the search operation, and the given search
times are adjusted accordingly.
3.2.4
Experiment 4 - Model Validation by Fire Scenario
The last experiment of this project involves a relatively full fire scenario for an overall model
validation of FBIM. The scenario was devised to be analogous to the cases simulated in [35]
to some extent. It was developed to include the activities of the following two phases in a
general intervention process: the investigation and initiation phase and the main intervention
phase. Due to the limitations of a fire drill and the training facilities, it was not possible
to include the first phase of a general intervention in a realistic manner. Moreover, as the
last phase in the current state of FBIM, is mostly conceptual, it was omitted from the fire
scenario. Thereby, the main focus of this validation is on the activities from arrival to the fire
46
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:54==
scene, to fire control/extinguishing and search and rescue. This section will only provide a
brief description of the experiment and the reader is referred to Appendix A, for a complete
description. The documented results of the experiment are provided in Appendix 3.3.
Experiment Description
The fire scenario, inspired by the FBIM simulations in [35], involves a fire in an electrical vehicle
in a parking cellar. In this case, there was no automatic fire detection of suppression system
present, but the fire was discovered and the fire brigade was notified by a fire warden at the
premises. The fire brigade dispatches an appliance along with an OIC and five firefighters (1
driver and two SHTs).
Upon arrival, at which point the fire scenario begins, the OIC and the firefighters have already
donned their safety equipment. The OIC is greeted by the fire warden, from which the OIC is
briefed about the location and extent of the fire, the entry and access points, and that the fire
warden is unsure whether there are people inside requiring rescue. After the communication
with the fire warden, the OIC instructs and initiates the firefighters, i.e. the activity of relay
and receive orders.
SHT 1, is instructed to set up a dry-pipe attack by the use of high-rise packs and when ready,
advance directly and exclusively to the fire compartment to initiate fire control. SHT 2 is
instructed to establish the water supply to the dry-pipe system and to the appliance, and when
complete they are to report back to the OIC. These activities follow the same procedures as
in Section 2.1.2 and experiment 2. When the SHTs have been initiated, the OIC performs a
perimeter reconnaissance around the premises, to assess the situation. This step of the fire
scenario is illustrated in Fig. 3.8
When SHT 2 has completed their initial tasks, they are instructed to perform the search and
rescue operation of the premises, equipped with an HP hose. Meanwhile, SHT 1 ensures that
the fire remains under control and begins ventilating the fire compartment. When all the
premises have been searched, as there were no people in need of rescue, SHT 2 is to support
SHT 1. At this point, there are now two nozzles on the fire, and fire extinguishing is initiated
Figure 3.8: Sketch of experiment 4, the first attack line of the fire scenario
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
47
==PAGE:55==
Figure 3.9: Sketch of experiment 4, the second attack line of the fire scenario
which marks the end of the scenario. The part of the fire scenario is illustrated in Fig. 3.9 and
3.10. The red path is that of SHT 1, while the green is of SHT 2.
To compare with the FBIM simulation, the following milestones are outlined:
Time for determination of fire location
Time to relay and receive orders
Time for OIC to assess fire scene
Time to establish a water supply, SHT 2 is ready for a new assignment
Time to establish the first attack line, SHT 1 is ready to advance
Time to establish a second attack line, SHT 2 is ready to perform search and rescue
Time to initiate fire control, SHT 1 has reached the fire compartment
Time to initiate fire extinguishing, SHT 2 has completed the search and rescue and has
reached the fire compartment
The times of all external activities are recorded and documented by a series of stationary
cameras. The internal activities of the two SHTs, seen in Fig. 3.10, are documented by
two observers, following each team. When the fire scenario has been completed, all relevant
distances are measured and documented.
The drill is to be carried out at ’normal fire ground speed’ and the involved firefighters are
not to be briefed on the details of the scenario. However, to manage the development of the
scenario, the OIC was briefed on the overall scope and milestones of the experiment. This was
to ensure as many comparable elements to FBIM as possible.
48
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:56==

SHT 1 - red path, SHT 2 - green path
3.3
Data Analysis
This section is dedicated to describing how the final results are obtained. This entails, how the
measurements are performed and what associated error can be expected. Furthermore, the
measurements of the activities considered in the experiments require a statistical representation.
As the sample pools of this project are too small for any thorough statistical analysis, this
project only covers a basic description. All data collected in this report is documented in a
spreadsheet as seen in Appendix B.
3.3.1
Error Estimation
In this project, there are two main types of data; temporal and spatial values, each with distinct
inherent errors as a result of the measurement. These errors will in this section be assessed to
identify the minimal limitations of the final results.
The spatial parameters, either as distances, hose length, or area, are needed to convert
time duration of activities into FBIM comparable and applicable units. For the distances,
the acceptance criteria of the length of a fire hose used by CARFA is assumed applicable.
This implies that all hose lengths are considered to be associated with a minimal error of
δL = ± 0.5 m/15 mhl. This corresponds to 33 centimeters per 10-meter length of measurement.
This error is assumed applicable to all distances used in this project. All distances are provided
in meters and are considered with only one decimal.
For the areas needed to estimate the search speeds, the widths and lengths of each compartment
were measured by laser, providing rather accurate results. However, any objects or furnishing,
etc. within the compartment are not included in the estimates. Still, in light of the complexity
of search operations, the error associated with the area measurements is considered neglectable
For the temporal values, stop-watches were used in the first experiment giving the times of
duration for each activity. These activities were rather simple and very well-defined. All other
data were collected by video recordings, and the activities were not so well-defined. Thus, it
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
49
==PAGE:57==
required an identification of the start and end times based on estimates.
To demonstrate this challenge, refer to the activities of P1 and V1 DK, i.e. the task of firefighter
no. 4 in a standard attack line cf. Table 3.2. Here, the challenge entails the discussion of
e.g. whether the removal of the hydrant equipment is solely about the actual removal, or if
it should also encompass finding the tools and moving about the appliance, to the specific
cabinet. Or, related to the specific case, entail the discussion of whether to include moving the
hydrant equipment to the end of the appliance before the transition to the activity of V1 DK.
Likewise, the challenge warrants a discussion of whether the activity of laying, connecting, and
charging the feeding line, V1 DK, should include the hand-over of the B-hose clutch by FF no.
4 to the driver, and walking back to the B-baskets, before heading towards the hydrant. Or
whether it should only be defined from the moment the B-hose is connected to the appliance.
Similar challenges arise across many of the activities documented by the video recordings.
As the data is intended for use in FBIM simulations, the flow within the FBIM charts has served
as a guideline in defining the borders of the specific activities. Along with the aim to represent
the reality, the following definitions were identified:
The removal of equipment: From the moment the given FF is near the appliance with
the intention to remove the equipment. Till the moment the FF transits to their next
task
The laying and connecting of hoses: From the moment the given FF is near the beginning
of the line or branch, with the necessary equipment and hoses, and the intention of
establishing the hose connection.
Till the moment where the given connection is
established and the FF is ready to transit to the activity of charging
The charging of hoses: From the moment the given FF has established the connection
with the intention of charging the line or branch. Till the water has reached the end of
the line and clear pressure built-up in the whole line is present
By these guidelines, estimates of start and end times of all the activities, documented by
recordings, were obtained. They are considered to provide a justifiable representation of the
activities in an FBIM context. This approach ensures that the entire flow of the intervention is
represented in the data, i.e. the entire process of establishing a feeding line is accounted for
within O1, P1, and V1 DK and similar. This is also in alignment with the degree of details in
FBIM simulations, where e.g. the small horisontal movements around the appliance to where
the pump or tools are located, are not directly accounted for.
All the start and end times are thereby based on an individual assessment, utilising either a
stopwatch or video recordings with 30 frames per second. This implies that the duration is
bound to inherent errors of both the start and end time assessments and estimates. The total
inherent error for the duration estimates is assessed to be: δt,i = ± 3 sec. This represents an
error of 1.5 seconds at each point of measurement and is considered a conservative minimum
error of the temporal duration values obtained in this project. This error reflects the undefined
nature of the activities and accounts for e.g. a firefighter standing straight, when the start
time is documented, or leaning in, almost grabbing the equipment.
3.3.2
Statistical Analysis
Besides the inherent error introduced by the process of measurement, it is natural to expect
some variation in the execution of the activities that the temporal values represent. This implies
the need for a statistical model. In this project, it is assumed that all the activities can be
represented by a normal distribution. This is a very crude assumption, and it can be argued
50
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:58==
that the activities would follow another distribution such as the log-normal distribution as in
[14]. However, due to the small statistical population of the data collected in this project,
a thorough statistical analysis including validation of the statistical distributions, would be
unproductive. This assumption is thereby considered appropriate for this preliminary analysis.
Nonetheless, each activity is assessed by its mean value, µ, and its standard deviation, σ,
according to [38]:
µ = 1
N
N
X
i=1
ti
∧
σ2 = 1
N
N
X
i=1
(ti −µ)2
(3.1)
Where N is the sample size and ti is the data point of the i’th measurement.
In some cases, multiple cameras were used to estimate a single activity. For these cases, a
mean and standard deviation is provided for that single activity and experiment. This sample
pool is considered ’dependent’, and illustrates the challenge of estimating the duration values
of the activity. Moreover, some activities were conducted and documented several times
throughout the experiments. For these cases, the mean values from each experiment were
used to estimate the overall mean and standard deviation of the activity. This sample pool is
considered ’independent’, and represents a statistical variation of the activity.
Thus, the final results will either be composed of a single measurement, a mean and a standard
deviation of a single measurement by multiple cameras, or a mean and a standard deviation of
mean values from several measurements.
3.4
Software Development
One of the main objectives of this project was to develop a digital tool for running FBIM
simulations. In this section, the main ideas and principles behind the developed software are
presented along with brief instructions on its use.
The developed software is based on the programming language ’Python’. The aim was to
create a program to run through the FBIM charts and keep track of all the time estimates.
Based on the learnings from [35], besides the program’s primary function, it was developed
with an emphasis on user interaction and simulation documentation.
3.4.1
Software Architecture
By utilising the concepts of object-oriented programming, a library script with the FBIM data,
and a chart script for each FBIM flowchart were created. They were designed such that each
chart can be associated with a file containing the user inputs, i.e. the answers to the boxes
in the FBIM flowchart. The aim was to implement the user inputs to automatically refer to
the FBIM data while keeping all the inputs simple and relevant. For instance, in the case of
Table Q, containing the horisontal travel speeds of the firefighters, the natural inputs would be
the type of traveling (Q1-Q4) and the traveling distance. The program should also be able to
cover a case of multiple types of traveling each associated with a given distance.
While the library script represents the FBIM dataset in [3], and the chart scripts represent
the FBIM flowcharts in [2], the main script represents the case-specific ’Chain of Events’ as
the one illustrated in Fig. 2.11 Therefore, to run an FBIM simulation with the developed
program, a main script needs to be created. This script should reflect the chain of events of
the intervention and all the given trajectories, tying all the charts, input files, and the library
together. By the chosen methodology, the script would be able to collect all the different
actions and milestones and present them in a Gantt chart. However, this implies that the user
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design

is required to perform some hard coding before use. In the following sections, the principles
behind each of these concepts are presented. The aim is to allow the reader to use the program
to run FBIM simulations from their own main scripts.
3.4.1.1
The FBIM Library Script
The FBIM library is designed to reflect the data types and the required simulation parameters
supplied by the user. The script is based on the inbuilt definitions of functions in ’Python’.
By ’import’, the library and the functions, each representing an FBIM table, are accessible to
the chart scripts. Each function is tailored to the chart and data type it represents.
The data types are summarised in Table 3.3, categorised with respect to their features and
input requirements. The main features are:
No type required - this feature indicates that the user is not required to choose an element
of a table. This category contains all the single-element tables of FBIM.
Type required - this feature indicates that the user is required to choose an element of a
table. This category contains all the multiple-element tables of FBIM.
Time value to be supplied - this feature indicates that the user is required to input a time
estimate. This category contains all the tables of FBIM with elements of no value.
Action parameter(s) to be supplied - this feature indicates that the user is required to
input an action parameter e.g. walking distance, hose length, search area, or number of
52
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:60==
doors. This category contains the tables of FBIM with elements not directly representing
a time duration.
Statistical model provided - this feature implies that the data is represented by a statistical
distribution, allowing the user to apply a specific percentile. This category contains the
tables of FBIM with elements represented by a mean and a standard deviation, which in
this program is modeled by a normal distribution
Calculation required - this feature implies that some form of simple calculation and
conversion of the FBIM data, besides addition, is required by the program.
Multiple input set required - this feature indicates the need to allow for multiple sets of
inputs regarding types and action parameter(s). This category contains all the tables
of FBIM, which represent activities that might be needed several times, with different
action values. This category contains i.a. the walking of an FF by Table Q, the hose
connections in Table V and X, or for the search operations in Table Y.
By the categorisation in Table 3.3, the needed structure for each FBIM table in the library can
Figure 3.11: Cut-outs from the FBIM library script developed in this project
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
53
==PAGE:61==
be identified. The examples shown in Fig. 3.11 illustrate the general structure of the proposed
solutions to each feature. For instance, the case of No Type Required is illustrated in Tables
G and M, as there is no dependence on a type input. The opposite case of Type Required is
illustrated in Tables D, F, and V. The user chooses the type by the element IDs, such as D1
or F2. The code is designed to identify the ID number by its definitive second place in the
arrangement of e.g. the input D2. For the tables, that are more complex in structure such as
Table V, two numbers are required to indicate the element type, e.g. V12 or V30.
The examples of Time Values Supplied are illustrated in Tables D and G and are designed to
simply return the value of time duration in seconds provided by the user inputs. In cases of
Action Parameter, as illustrated in Tables F and V, the user is required to input e.g. a driving
distance in meters in F2, or the hose length of each type in Table V. For cases of Statistical
Model, as seen in Tables M and V, the user is required to input a given percentile of the specific
dataset. Then, by the in-built functions from ’numpy’, representing a normal distribution based
on a pool size of 10.000, the program returns the data value at the given percentile. By these
representations of the normal distribution with no specific seed, small deviations in results for
identical simulations are now bound to occur. These introduced computational errors, can be
minimised by either defining a specific seed or at best, upscaling the pool size. The examples
of calculation Required are illustrated in Tables F and V. In these cases, simple mathematical
operations are required to compute the final time duration.
Until now, all inputs to the library have consisted of integers, either in the form of type integer
values, time values, or action values. This implies that the inputs to the program are limited to
temporal integer values in seconds, and spatial integer values in meters, square meters, etc.,
which is deemed consistent with the level of detail appropriate for FBIM simulations. However,
in the cases of Multiple Sets, as illustrated in Table V, the input form has been changed to a
’list’. This allows the user to specify any number of sets of a ’type’ associated with an ’action
value. This ensures that a high level of generalisation is achieved in the relevant tables, and
makes the structures of e.g. Table V applicable in the program. For this reason, it is important
to note that these inputs must be given in a specific format of a list with strings, e.g. of
[’V12’,’V42’] associated with the hose lengths [’30’,’30’].
Using similar solutions to the remaining FBIM tables, the Python library is established. Each
table can be accessed by any chart as illustrated by the case of Table V:
fbim lib.table v(type=TYPE INPUT, hose lens=LENGTH INPUT, percentile=P.tile INPUT)
3.4.1.2
The FBIM Charts Scripts
Each chart program is designed to reflect the logic of the FBIM charts they represent. This is
achieved by utilising the mechanism ’class’, to define the FBIM charts and the specific flows.
Within each class or chart program, the paths through the FBIM charts, are governed by the
event boxes of the FBIM charts, defined by ’def’. To relate the event boxes to the input files,
an index parameter is used. This ensures that if e.g. Box 6 is activated, the inputs related to
Box 6 are accessed. This is achieved by hard coding, as each progression of indexation needs
to be tailored to the specific structure of the input file. This implies, that the user is restrained
from making changes to the input files, other than changing the actual inputs.
In general, there are three main types of event-boxes in FBIM. The first type is simple yes/no
questions, the second type involves a direct addition of a time value, while the third type involves
an addition of a time value based on another FBIM chart (and input file). To demonstrate
the suggested solutions to each of these types of event boxes, the reader is referred to Chart
10 of the FBIM. Each chart can be separated into two parts, where the first part consists of
54
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:62==
Figure 3.12: Initialisation of the script in Chart 10, ’Chart10.py’
setting up the prerequisites or initialising the script such as importing the necessary libraries,
defining the class constructor, defining the default values, reading the input file, and extracting
the input values. This part is illustrated in Fig. 3.12 for the case of Chart 10.
The second part contains all the event boxes of the FBIM charts and defines the logical path
throughout the program. Examples of each of the three types of event boxes are given in Fig.
3.13 for the cases of Chart 10, event-box 2, 4, and 7.
In event box 2, it is seen how the program handles a yes or no operation. It is seen how the
program progresses to box 7 when answering ’No’, and the hard coding of the corresponding
indexation requiring the addition of 13. This number is, as mentioned, associated with the
specific number of lines between box 2 and 7 in the given input file.
In event box 4, it is seen how the program is designed to deal with multiple tables, each
requiring multiple sets of inputs. In box 4, three tables are activated, and the program is
designed to sequentially go through the tables and corresponding input values. Moreover, it
is illustrated how the chart refers to the FBIM data library, and how the lists of each set of
inputs are extracted from the input file.
In event box 7, it is demonstrated how the program is designed to incorporate other FBIM
charts into the current chart. In this case, Chart 11 is activated, and associated with the second
input file, within the list of input files.
Some of the flowcharts defined in the FBIM manual are of more conceptual use than compu-
tational use. Therefore, to implement the flowcharts in a working simulation program, some
modifications were required. The following flowcharts have been modified:
