be adjusted. Currently, this feature is only available in the developed program by adopting the
main script, and running the given charts as a distinct trajectory.
3.4.2
The Main Script
The library and the chart scripts constitute the main generic parts for running FBIM simulations.
However, to run a specific case, with a certain sequence through the charts each associated
with a distinct input file, a main script is needed. As mentioned, the main script needs to
be designed to reflect the case-specific chain of events of the intervention and all the given
trajectories. Moreover, the purpose of the main script is also to process the results, making it
easy for the user to identify given milestones for the assessment of activities or analyse the
intervention process as a whole. This is solved by the visualisation of the data in a Gantt chart
and a print-out of user-defined milestones.
Figure 3.14: Initialisation of main script
The process of setting up the prerequisites or initialise
the main script primarily consists of an ’import’ of
the necessary chart script and the Python library
’matplotlib’, while preallocating the applied pa-
rameters. To store the results, three parameters are
needed in the format of lists, and one parameter in
the format of a dictionary, as seen in Fig. 3.14. The
Gantt chart at the end of the script is constructed by
the three parameters: ’start vals’, ’dur vals’,
and ’labels’. As their names suggest, they contain
the start values, the duration values, and a given
label, for each of the activities sought to be displayed in the chart.
For each step in the chain of events, the execution of a chart script is performed by:
chartX = ChartX.Chart X(time=TIME INPUT,input file=["INPUT FILE 1","INPUT FILE 2",...])
Where ’ChartX’, represents a given Chart X, with specific starting time: ’TIME INPUT’,
and input files, stored in a list: ["INPUT FILE 1","INPUT FILE 2",...]. The result from
running the given chart, i.e. the accumulated time estimate, from the given starting time and
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
57
==PAGE:65==
based on the user input, provided in the input files, is then stored in the variable ’chartX’.
The corresponding results could then be stored by:
labels.append(’Chart X’)
start vals.append(TIME INPUT)
dur vals.append(chartX-TIME INPUT)
Each trajectory of the intervention process is thereby implemented by specific stored results in
these lists.
The parameter ’milestones’ is mainly intended to highlight and store specific results and pro-
cesses, as the accumulated time estimate is not directly readable from the Gantt chart parameters.
In this dictionary, a label can be associated with temporal values as: milestones[’LABEL’]
= [value], which can then be printed at the end of the simulation.
This data processing and visualisation is performed as illustrated in Fig. 3.15. By this approach,
the arrangement of the labels, dur vals, and start vals corresponds to the order of display
in the Gantt chart. Furthermore, they can be associated with a distinct color and displayed
on a specific time scale as seen in xticks=np.arrange(0,1020+60,60. Lastly, any desired
milestones, or as in this case, time duration values, are written to a .txt file, and the Gantt
chart is saved in .png format
Within the main script, certain dependencies between specific activities also need to be calculated
and checked. For instance, during a dry-pipe attack, the team with the attack branch cannot
pressurise their hoses, and thus enter the fire compartment, before the water connection to the
dry-pipe system is established.
To this introduction of the developed program, a series of corresponding templates of the library
script, the chart scripts, the input files, and the main scripts are available in Appendix C, and
Figure 3.15: Result documentation, and Gantt chart creation
58
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:66==
submitted along with this thesis. By these, it is expected that the reader is equipped with the
prerequisites to use and modify the program to run their own FBIM simulation. In the next
section, the developed software is utilised to run the FBIM simulation, corresponding to the
fourth experiment, which will also serve as a practical demonstration of the software.
3.5
FBIM Simulations
The FBIM simulations in this project are intended for comparisons with the fire scenario of
experiment four. A set of three simulations has been devised, and the process is described in
this section. The three simulations are selected to reflect specific features of the assumptions in
FBIM simulations and demonstrate the use of the data collected in this project. All simulations
are based on mean values and roughly estimated spatial values, as it would have been done in
any pre-fire simulation process.
The main features of each simulation case are as follows:
Case 1 - FBIM data:
– First attack branch by V2.2 and V4.2
– Second attack branch by V2.2 only
– Dry-pipe double connection by V2.1 and V4.1
– Feeding line by V1.1
– Search and rescue simulated by ’secondary search’ Y2 by an estimated search area
Case 2 - FBIM data:
– First attack branch by V2.2 and V4.2
– Second attack branch by Q3 and T2
– Dry-pipe double connection by V2.1 and V4.1
– Feeding line by V1.1
– Search and rescue simulated by ’primary search’ Q3 by an estimated perimeter
search
Case 3 - DK data:
– First attack branch by P2 DK, T1.2 DK, V2.3 DK, and V4.3 DK
– Second attack branch by P3 DK, T2 DK, and V6 DK
– Dry-pipe double connection by P6 DK, V2.1 DK and V4.1 DK
– Feeding line by P1 DK, V1 DK
– Search and rescue simulated by ’secondary search’ Y1 DK of the full compartment
area
Cases 1 and 2 were devised to reflect the challenge of the underlying assumption of secondary
versus primary search within FBIM. They were developed with hindsight of the events of the
actual fire scenario in question. During the conduction of the experiment, it was observed that
the search team performed their task best described by a combination of primary and secondary
search. Sometimes they would walk, sometimes they would creep, and other times something in
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
59
==PAGE:67==
between. Therefore, it is expected that the two different search settings in the FBIM simulation
will indicate an upper and lower bound for the search and rescue operation simulation.
Figure 3.16: Overview of the ’Chain of
Events’ of the fire scenario in experiment 4
The first two cases also reflect the challenge of
simulating an HP attack. Since there are no obvi-
ous choices for this in the current FBIM dataset,
the first case assumes that the establishing of the
HP attack line can be described with V2.2 only.
This assumption entails the removal and connec-
tion of a 38mm hose while charging of the hose is
omitted. The assumption of omitting the charg-
ing part of the activity is considered reasonable
due high pressure involved.
Case 3 is based on the dataset collected in this
project. The data collected in this project are
conceptually directly applicable to FBIM simu-
lations. However, due to the small sample size
in this project, the data does not represent the
activity with the appropriate statistical accuracy
required for a valid simulation. This simulation
is merely indicative and for academic purposes.
All cases will be compared with the actual values
from the fire scenario.
To construct the main script to perform the FBIM
simulations, the chain of events is identified, as
illustrated in Fig. 3.16. It contains the phases and
the flow of activities observed in the experiment,
see Section 3.2.4, and it defines the structure of
the simulation. The structure is general enough
to be used for all three simulation cases, as the
differences are reflected in the input files. All sim-
ulations are performed with mean values, and the
temporal and spatial parameters are estimated by
the methods of Section 3.3, after the completion
of the experiment.
To demonstrate the set-up of the simulations and
reveal the underlying assumptions, the activities during the fire scenario are separated into
phases and elaborated in the following sections. They are presented in the light of simulation
case 1. The remaining cases are created by modifying the relevant user inputs and these
changes will be further outlined in Section 4.2.
3.5.1
The Investigation and Initiation Phase
The first phase of the fire scenario is commenced in Chart 5, representing the time for initial
determination of the fire location. By the description of the experiment in Section 3.2.4, the
scenario begins with the appliance and crew arriving on the scene. The crew had already
dismounted the appliance and donned their safety equipment. There is no need to travel
through the site, the fire is not visible, and the premises are occupied. There is no hindrance
due to evacuation, no security procedures to perform, and there is a fire warden present. By
these circumstances, the path taken in Chart 5 only involves the activation of Table H, before
60
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:68==
Figure 3.17: Cut-outs of Chart 5 from the FBIM main script and the input files
it is completed. The value of H1 is selected, which will be the first contribution to the timeline.
In Fig. 3.17, the two input files to the given case are documented. Furthermore, the figure
illustrates how the results are obtained and stored.
As Chart 5 is complete and the OIC has determined the location of the fire, the next natural
step is to brief the crew and instruct them on their tasks. As mentioned, FBIM does not contain
this element explicitly, but by the methodology in [35], the value of C1 has been selected to
represent the activity of ’Relay and Receive Orders’. The value of 60 seconds is added manually
in the main script as illustrated at the top of Fig. 3.18.
When the crew has been briefed, they join together into Smoke Helmet Teams (SHTs) and
begin their primary tasks. As they prepare, the OIC begins to assess the fire situation by Chart
7. The experimental set-up dictates that the fire location and extent are not obvious without
reconnaissance, and the given building is not greater than three storeys in height. By these
conditions, the path taken in Chart 7, leads to Box 4. Here, the OIC performs a perimeter
reconnaissance of the fire-affected area, after which they conclude, that there is no need for
additional resources. The process is documented in Fig. 3.18, illustrating the input file used in
Chart 7 along with how the results are obtained and stored.
This concludes the investigation and initiation phase, as each SHT has commenced with their
primary tasks.

3.5.2
The Main Intervention Phase
When the crew has been briefed, they split up and prepare to move to their respective set-up
areas, described by Chart 8 and sub-Charts 6 and 9. In the case of SHT 1, they move towards
the set-up area near the dry-pipe outlet after having removed a high-rise pack each. The set-up
area of SHT 2 is defined to be near the appliance. In this case, this activity only involves the
removal of the hydrant equipment, which is assumed equivalent to dry-pipe equipment, and
the flush of the hydrant. The part of the fire scenario is illustrated in Fig. 3.8, and the process
is documented in Fig. 3.19, illustrating how the results are obtained and stored, with the input
files of Fig. 3.20 and 3.22.
By the given experimental conditions, the path taken in Chart 8, for SHT 1, activates Box 6 to

In the case of SHT 2, each firefighter has their separate tasks. FF no. 3 is to establish the

Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design

both to report back by the appliance when complete. To describe these two distinct activities,
and to account for any waiting time before SHT 2 is reestablished, Chart 10 is utilised on each
of the firefighters, as illustrated in 3.21. Each simulation needs to be associated with distinct
input files, documented in Fig. 3.24 and 3.25.
At this point of the intervention process, two simulation controls are needed. First, it must
be ensured that both FF no. 3 and no. 4 are complete and have returned to the appliance
before they are deployed again. This is evidently not the case in real fire scenarios, as they
would just begin or support other activities. However, this cannot be captured by the current
state of FBIM, as there is no way to account for the time to e.g. single-handedly begin the
rollout of the HP-hose. Therefore, for the simulation and modeling purposes, the waiting time
is introduced, as illustrated in Fig. 3.26.
Secondly, it must be ensured that before SHT 1 can pressurise its attack branch, FF no. 3 must
have established and charged at least one hose connection. The control in the simulation is a
simplified condition, as it is assumed equivalent to compare when SHT 1, should be ready to
move towards the fire compartment, with when FF no. 3 should be complete with the double
hose connection to the dry-pipe system. This corresponds to the accumulated times of the
respective Chart 10 when subtracting the contribution from Box 8, i.e. Chart 9. The control is
illustrated in Fig. 3.27.
Figure 3.26: Cut-outs from the FBIM main script, estimating the time for SHT 2
to complete their first task
Figure 3.27: Cut-outs from the FBIM main script, estimating the time for SHT 1 to be ready
to move towards the fire compartment
66
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:74==
At this point of the simulation, the first attack branch is established and by the end of Chart 10,
SHT 1 has reached the fire compartment and is ready to engage the fire and initiate fire control.
Moreover, SHT 2 has completed its primary task and is reestablished near the appliance, ready
to be deployed. SHT 2 is then instructed to establish an HP attack line and conduct the search
and rescue operation of the premises, before assisting SHT 1 by engaging the fire and initiating
fire extinguishing. This part of the intervention is illustrated in Fig. 3.9 and 3.10.
Therefore, the next step of the simulation is to introduce fire control in Chart 15 and the search
and rescue in Chart 12. However, as revealed in [35], Chart 15 is mainly conceptual and, for
the sake of simulations, does not offer any more insights into the intervention process. This is
especially true for cases, such as this, where the attack branches have already been established,
and the time to extinguish the fire, etc. cannot be estimated.
The experiment is devised to represent fire in an electrical vehicle, and as demonstrated in
[35], the extinguishing process is not well-defined. Therefore, it is assumed that one attack
is equivalent to the process of fire control, and two attacks are equivalent to the process of
fire extinguishing. This is also reflected in the experimental description, as the experiment
is concluded when two nozzles are on the fire. By these arguments, it is in this case, not
meaningful to utilise Chart 15 in the simulation. However, by Table 2.1, this assumption
corresponds to a fire with a maximal HRR of 5.93 MW, referring to the bounds for a single
C attack with respect to fire control. At this maximal HRR of the fire, decay is by Table 2.1
possible for both two C attacks, or a C and an HP attack. The investigation of [39], indicates
that the max HRR of the fire in the tested electrical family car, lies around 5 MW, while the
tested electrical vans yielded a max HRR of 6 and 7 MW. For comparison, the maximal HRR
of a fire in a normal car has been found to 3.5 MW [40]. The normal car was however much
older, which limits any direct comparison. In any case, the assumption of a maximal HRR of a
fire in an electrical vehicle of 5.93 MW is considered reasonable.
The process of establishing the HP attack, by SHT 2 is simulated by utilising Chart 10 once
again. The contributions are again made in Box 4 and Box 8. In the simulation, is it assumed
that the attack branch is established on the ground and near the appliance (thus no need for
firefighter travel by Table Q or T), and when charged, SHT 2 moves towards the entry point,
described by Box 8. Thus, by the end of this Chart 10, SHT 2 is ready to commence the search
and rescue operation. The simulation is performed as illustrated in Fig. 3.28, with the input
files documented in Fig. 3.29.
When the HP attack line has been established, the search and rescue operation by SHT 2 can
commence. This is described by Chart 12 and by the experimental conditions, the path taken
yields only one contribution. In Box 6 where either the floor area or perimeter etc. of the
premises are estimated, to compute the time of the search operation. The reason for choosing
the path towards Box 6 rather than Box 7, is to account for the traveling on internal staircases
during the search. However, the application of Table Y in either Box 6 or 7 does not entail any
differences in terms of simulation. The simulation is performed as seen in Fig. 3.30, where the
input file is also documented. When the search and rescue operation has been conducted, SHT
2 engages the fire and assists SHT 1. The combined water application of the two nozzles is
Figure 3.28: Cut-outs of Chart 10 from the FBIM main script, describing the second attack
line by SHT 2

==PAGE:75==
considered sufficient to ensure fire decay, and the simulation is completed.

Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:76==
Chapter 4
Results
This chapter is devoted to presenting all the obtained results of this project. During the
first three experiments, the 27 distinct firefighting activities, outlined in Table 3.2, have been
documented. These results comprise the data collection of this project, and the data are
collected to either validate corresponding FBIM data or to implement and outline activities
that are not represented in FBIM. The results of these experiments are provided in Section 4.1.
During the last experiment, a fire scenario was conducted from which eight milestones were
identified and documented. The results of this experiment are provided in Section 4.2, along
with the results of the associated FBIM simulations.
The following sections utilise the measure of relative error (RE) to quantify the discrepancy
between the documented results and the corresponding FBIM values. When comparing the
activities in the data collection of this project, the relative error is computed as:
RE = abs (µFBIM −µDK)
µDK
(4.1)
Where RE represents the relative error between the expected value µFBIM, and the measured
value µDK. When comparing the milestones documented in the fire scenario with corresponding
FBIM simulations, the expected values are those documented, while the measured values are
those obtained by the simulation.
4.1
Data Collection
In this section, the results of the data collection are presented. For each of the three experiments,
intended to collect data on distinct intervention activities, the results are summarised, and, if
any, compared to those equivalent in the FBIM dataset.
4.1.1
Experiment 1 - The Physical Test
The results of the first experiment, during the firefighters’ physical test, are summarised in
Table 4.1. The results are provided as means and standard deviations of the duration of each
activity, included in the experiment. As evident by the given pool sizes, each result is comprised
of at least ten independent measurements.
Each activity included in this experiment represents a traveling speed. Therefore, all the
obtained duration values are converted into speeds, by the conversion parameters given in Table
4.1. After the conversion, the means and standard deviations are computed, and provided in
Table 4.2. These obtained values constitute the final results of this experiment. The equivalent

The final results, provided in Table 4.2, refer to the activity IDs presented in Table 3.2. However,
the first two activities (Q3 w/o and Q3 w/) refer to the walking speed of a firefighter in BA
without and with equipment, respectively. This distinction is not made in FBIM, which is why
they are both compared to the same value of Q3. Similarly, the ascend of stairs by a firefighter
in BA without equipment (T1.2 DK) is not represented in FBIM and is therefore compared
with T1.
4.1.2
Experiment 2 - The Danish Standard Attack Lines
In this section, all of the results obtained during experiment 2 are presented. Experiment 2
consists of four distinct firefighter drills, and the results obtained from each drill are initially
provided. Based on these, a full summary of the final results, obtained in this experiment, is
presented.
The measured duration values of the given activities are from each drill provided in Tables 4.3,
4.5, 4.7, and 4.9. These results are provided as means and standard deviations of each activity,
referring to the activity IDs presented in Table 3.2. The intention for the data collection is for
each activity evident by ’Val.’ or ’Impl.’. These categories refer to the intention to validate
current activities or implement new activities.
Some of the activities included in this experiment represent simple duration values to be added
in FBIM simulations, while others represent traveling speeds or speeds of establishing hose
connections. Therefore, the duration values of these are converted into FBIM comparable
and compatible units. For those activities requiring a conversion, the associated conversion
parameters are also provided.
70
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:78==
The measurements from each drill are considered dependent, but as indicated by Ncam, some
activities were registered by more than one camera. This illustrates the inherent uncertainty of
the measurements and the activities themselves and enables the computation of a standard
deviation. The standard deviation could, in these cases, be used as a measure of the complexity
of the activities.
The final results are for each drill provided in Tables 4.4, 4.6, 4.8, and 4.10. The values
constitute the final results of this experiment and are compared to equivalent counterparts from
the FBIM dataset [3], by the measure of relative error. In general, those activities in the ’Impl.’
category do not have equal FBIM counterparts. However, the closest equivalent counterpart, if
any, is provided.
E.g. for the case of V1 DK, the FBIM equivalent values used in the comparison, are those
of V1.2, i.e. a hose of 65mm in diameter and not 75mm as a B-hose. Similarly, V2.1 DK
and V4.1 DK is compared with V2.1 and V4.1, and V2.2 DK and V4.2 DK with V2.2 and
V4.2. Moreover, the cases of V2.3 DK and V4.3 DK are compared to the equivalent FBIM
values of V2.2 and V4.2. These values therefore represent different hose diameters but are
still considered relatively equivalent. Similarly, T4.1 DK is compared to T4, while T7 DK, the
speed of ascend on a staircase in turnout uniform w/ equipment, is compared to the closest
match of T1, ascend in BA w/ equipment.

4.1.3
Experiment 3 - Search & Rescue Operation
The results of the third experiment of this project are presented in this section. The third
experiment involved two distinct search and rescue operations.
They both involved the
removal/rescuing of a person, but the search was conducted in two different ways. The first
search was conducted by traditional means while the second was conducted with the aid of a
thermal camera.
The obtained results are provided in Table 4.12, including the relevant conversion parameters.
The zones in Table 4.12 refer to the search zones illustrated in Fig. 3.7, from which the
conversion parameters are estimated:
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
75
==PAGE:83==
A1 = 2.3 × 5.9m2 = 13.6m2
A2 = 4.6 × 2.2m2 = 10.1
A3 = 4.6 × 2.2m2 + 3.3 × 2.4m2 = 18.0m2
A4 = 4.6 × 2.2m2 = 10.1m2
A5 = 4.6 × 2.2m2 + 2.2 × 2.2m2 = 15.0m2
drescue = (0.7 + 2.2 + 1 + 2.3 + 2.5)m = 8.7m
Anot searched = 1 × 2.2m2 = 2.2m2
Where drescue is the estimated distance of the rescuing, while Anotsearched is an estimated area
of the premises not searched by the first team, because of the immediate rescue of the training
dummy in zone 5.

4.2
Model Validation
Experiment 4, the last experiment of this project, was intended to provide a framework for an
overall model validation of FBIM. Therefore, a fire scenario was devised, intended to represent
a simple and basic intervention process. During the fire scenario, distinct milestones were
documented to enable a comparison to FBIM simulations aimed at describing the specific fire
scenario. A series of three cases of FBIM simulations was created as described in Section 3.5.
The results of both the experiment and the three simulations are presented in this section.
The full summary of the results is provided in Table 4.14, where each milestone is associated with
an experimental result, documented during the fire actual fire scene. The corresponding results
from the three cases of FBIM simulations are also provided and compared to the experimental
by the relative error measure. However, to distinguish between over- and under-estimations, the
absolute value is omitted. Thereby, a negative RE indicates an overestimation and a positive
RE indicates an underestimation.
The first simulation case is documented in Table 4.16, and illustrated by the developed program
in a Gantt chart in Fig. 4.1a. The second simulation case is identical to the first, except for
the two last milestones as documented in Table 4.15, and illustrated in Fig. 4.1b. The last
simulation case utilises the obtained results from the first three experiments of this project, as
documented in Table 4.17, and illustrated in Fig. 4.1c.

Chapter 5
Discussion
This chapter contains a discussion of the results obtained in this project, presented in the
previous chapter. The aim is to provide the relevant context to the results and the framework
from which the conclusions are derived.
To assess the collected data, or ideally, validate the FBIM model, some kind of acceptance or
validation criteria needs to be defined. How accurate do the model, and the data parameters it
relies on, need to be? How accurate can they be? Modeling and simulations of fire brigade
intervention processes are in general associated with high uncertainty, due to the complex
activities themselves, but also due to the many circumstantial factors involved. Therefore, for
any model that aims to simulate such processes, a range of limits for the accuracy seems natural
to expect. To ensure high accuracy, large sample pools and thorough statistical analysis are
required, such as the one provided by FBIM. From such analysis, it would be possible to define
acceptance criteria based on a confidence interval or, as in FBIM, a specific high percentile of
the sample set.
As in most cases within structural engineering, accuracy is not the only objective. Since
underestimations can lead to intolerable failure scenarios, some level of ’certain safety’ needs
to be ensured. Certainty is often introduced by selecting high percentile values of data from
empirical tests, while safety is introduced by the means of safety factors in the theoretical models.
The same approach is considered applicable for FBIM, i.e. choosing e.g. the 90%-percentile
as suggested in the FBIM manual [2], while multiplying with appropriate safety factors along
the way. However, how these safety factors are introduced is still unknown. This warrants the
discussion of whether it is enough to multiply with a safety factor at the end of a simulation, if
there should be a safety factor for each chart or phase, and how large the factors should be.
To resolve this, more investigations are needed, but it would have to depend on the scope of
the simulations.
Certain safety is only required for well-documented models utilised to validate a design. At
this point of the FBIM development in Denmark, there is little need for certain safety, but
rather, a level of accuracy is required for the model to become well-documented. This project
is conceived with the aim to provide a preliminary study, to get one step closer. However, due
to the limited sample pools of the data in this project, which are the basis for a thorough
statistical analysis and validation, only qualitative assessments and indicative validations can
be justified.
To facilitate the indicative validations, an acceptance criterion of a maximal 30% deviation
between mean values, measured by the relative error, has been selected. This relatively high
acceptance criterion reflects the large uncertainty inherent in the activities and the results,
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
81
==PAGE:89==
along with the small sample pools. However, it does provide a guideline for the assessments and
the indicative validations. The mean of a statistical distribution represents the outcome 50% of
the time. If a given measurement of a similar but not identical activity, would correspond to a
data point of the 80%-percentile or higher, the deviations between the two mean values could
be substantial. Even more so, if the given measurement constitutes the only measurement
documented, at which, it has been used to represent the mean. Of the low sample pools
and the aim to indicatively validate the activities, this argument further supports the idea of
applying a high acceptance criterion. Moreover, if the relative error should be above 80%,
it is considered a clear indication that something is wrong and the activities might not be
comparable. Therefore, the comparison is considered inadmissible. By these rough limits, the
results are assessed by the following descriptors:
Indicatively validated: 0% < RE ≤30%
Not validated: 30% < RE ≤80%
Inadmissible:: 80% < RE ≤∞
In any case, more data needs to be collected to conclude anything definitively, but this will
provide an appropriate baseline to assess the results obtained in this project. These limits are
utilised in the assessment and discussion of the results, provided in the following sections.
5.1
Data collection
In this section, the results obtained during the data collection of individual activities in
experiments 1, 2, and 3, are assessed and discussed. The results are comprised of activities that
are intended for validation or implementation, as given in Table 3.2. The validation is based on
the relative error measure, comparing the mean of the results with the mean of the equivalent
FBIM counterparts. For some results intended for implementation, FBIM counterparts have
been presented to enable a comparison. Although close, the activities they represent are
considered significantly different, and the comparison is mainly to evaluate and assess, whether
these implementations and distinctions are reasonable
5.1.1
Experiment 1 - The Physical Test
The five activities represented by the results of experiment 1 are given in Table 4.2. Three of
the activities are intended for validation while two are intended for implementation. The latter
is, however, compared to the closest FBIM counterparts. Each activity and the results thereof
are assessed in this section.
Firefighter Horisontal Travel Speed - Table Q
The activity of Q3, dressed in turnout uniform in BA w/ or w/o equipment, is intended for
validation. The first two results presented in table 4.2, aim to represent this activity, but with
the distinction of ’w/’ and ’w/o’ equipment. By the acceptance criteria of this project, Q3
is considered indicatively validated by both the results of Q3 w/o and Q3 w/. The results
even show a significant resemblance for the case of Q3 w/ with a RE of 1.5%. However, the
results seem to indicate that Q3, might be too conservative for the case of ’w/o’ equipment,
illustrating that it might be beneficial to make the distinction. However, for this project, Q3 is
considered indicatively validated for Danish FBIM simulation.
Firefighter Stair Travel Speed - Table T
The activities of ascending and descending stairs in BA w/o equipment are represented by
T1.2 DK and T5 in Table 4.2. They are intended for implementation as these activities are
82
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:90==
not directly represented by the FBIM dataset [3]. The ascend of stairs in BA w/ equipment is
in FBIM represented by the data of T1, which is compared to the collected data of T1.2 DK.
Similarly, the descent of stairs in BA is represented by T5 and is compared to the collected
data of T1.2 DK. By these comparisons, the intent is to assess, whether these implementations
and distinctions are reasonable. By the results in Table 4.2, the RE is above 50% in both cases,
which indicates a significant discrepancy. The results of the experiment are more than twice
the size of those provided by FBIM. This might suggest that both T1 and T5 from FBIM
represent a case of w/ equipment. The results also indicate that utilising T1 and T5 in cases
w/o equipment might be too conservative, which suggests that FBIM should be extended to
include these distinctions. However, it can be argued that firefighter stair travel in BA w/o
equipment is a rare and special case, so it might not be sensible to investigate at this point of
the FBIM development. In any case, the two activities of T1.2 DK and T5 in this experiment
are considered not representative of T1 and T5 from FBIM. It is noted that the results of
T1.2 DK and T5 also indicate a significant discrepancy to the findings in [36] and [37].
Time for Search and Rescue - Table Y
The last activity of experiment 1, involves the activity of removing/rescuing a person, represented
by Y2 in Table 4.2. The intention is to validate the activity. By the results in Table 4.2, the
RE is above 90%. This indicates that the comparison is inadmissible, and the two activities
might not be comparable. However, the activity included in the experiment was a very basic
and crude representation. It involved dragging a large iron chain, which represented a person
of 70kg, 40m in a backward walk. Thereby, details of a realistic removal and rescuing of a
person, such as a pick-up, etc., are not captured. The results are therefore expected to show
some discrepancy to those provided by FBIM. Still, a 90% discrepancy is too large, and the
activities of Y2 in the experiment are considered not representative of Y2 from FBIM. Y2 can
therefore not be considered indicatively validated. However, it is notable that the data from
FBIM is comprised of a very small mean value and a corresponding standard deviation in the
same order of magnitude.
5.1.2
Experiment 2 - The Danish Standard Attack Lines
The 21 activities included in experiment 2, spread out among four distinct firefighter drills,
are summarised in Table 4.11. Here, the final results are provided, including a comparison to
equivalent FBIM counterparts, by the measure of relative error. For each drill, the obtained
results of activity duration values are provided in Tables 4.3, 4.5, 4.7, and 4.9. These provide
the measurements, on which the final results are based. The measurements are converted
into FBIM comparable and compatible units, and the results from each drill are compared to
equivalent FBIM counterparts in the Tables 4.4, 4.6, 4.8, and 4.10. These enable an evaluation
of each measurement, from which the final results in Table 4.11 are comprised. Each activity
and its results, included in experiment 2, are assessed in this section.
Time to Conduct Safety Procedures - Table O
The activity of flushing a hydrant before use is represented by O1 in FBIM. It has been included
in this experiment with the intention of validating the current value. The results provided in
Table 4.11 indicate a RE of nearly 80%. By this significant deviation and the acceptance criteria
of this project, this value is considered non-validated. However, by observing each individual
measurement a pattern is evident. The first measurement in Table 4.3 corresponds well to the
FBIM mean value, while each of the remaining measurements indicates a significantly reduced
duration. By going over all the video recordings, the problem was identified, which explains
this discrepancy. In the first drill, the hydrant was sealed with a strip and the firefighter was
thereby delayed compared to the others. In Denmark, it is quite common, that hydrants and
dry-pipes, etc. are sealed with locks or strips. Therefore, in hindsight, this should have been
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
83
==PAGE:91==
included in each of the measurements. In any case, it was not possible to validate the activity
represented by O1. However, it is notable how close the first measurement is.
Time to Remove Necessary Tools From Appliance - Table P
The activities to remove necessary tools from the appliance are in experiment 2, represented by
P1, P2, P3 DK, P4 DK, P5 , and P6 DK. The two first are already represented by equivalent
FBIM counterparts, while the remaining are not. They represent the aim of incorporating the
procedures of the Danish standard attack lines into FBIM. The results provided in Table 4.11,
indicate that P2, by the acceptance criterion of this project, is considered indicatively validated
for Danish FBIM simulation. However, this is a result based on a single measurement. For P1,
the RE is above 60%, and this activity can therefore not be considered indicatively validated.
It is notable that the results of the P-activities are rather similar, which might indicate that it
would be sufficient to generalise the activities further. The measurements of these data might
also be deluded by the fact, that the firefighters knew with more and more certainty where
the tools were located. It was observed that, even though the firefighters rotated positions
afterward, they were much more direct in the removal of the tools for each drill. This tendency
might explain the significant reduction in P1 and P3 DK between the two first firefighter drills.
Even though this tendency seems to converge, it might indicate the need to devise experiments
in the future with more independence.
Firefighter Horisontal Travel Speed - Table Q
The activities of Q1 and Q2, dressed in turnout uniform w/ and w/o equipment, are intended
for validation. The corresponding results presented in Table 4.11, aim to represent these
activities. By the acceptance criteria of this project, Q2 is considered indicatively validated
by the measurements of experiment 2, with an RE of nearly 10%. However, Q1 with an RE
of nearly 40% cannot be considered indicatively validated. The results further indicate that
in both cases, FBIM might be underestimating the travel time by providing a higher walking
speed. It is notable, that the results of Q1 and Q2 seem to be identical, indicating that the
walking speed is independent of equipment. This would contradict the expectations and the
FBIM data, illustrating the need for a larger sample pool.
Firefighter Stair Travel Speed - Table T
The activities of ascending stairs w/ HP-hose, w/ C-hose, and w/ equipment are represented
by T2, T4.1 DK, and T7 DK in Table 4.11, respectively. While T2, is intended for validation,
T4.1 DK and T7 DK are intended for implementation, as there are no direct FBIM counterparts
available. However, the ascend with a C-hose is compared to the nearest FBIM counterpart,
ascend with a 38mm diameter hose, which is smaller than a C-hose. By the results in Table
4.11 and the acceptance criterion of this project, T2 cannot be considered indicatively validated
as the RE is nearly 40%. However, this is based on a single measurement, and the comparison
is made with a mean value of a distribution with standard deviation of relatively comparable
magnitude. The result further indicates a larger traveling speed than its FBIM counterpart.
When comparing T4.1 DK based on two measurements with T4, the RE is above 50%. This
indicates the need to incorporate the Danish hoses into FBIM and suggests that utilising T4 to
represent T4.1 DK would yield a larger, and therefore less conservative, traveling speed. The
implementation of T7 DK is suggested due to the lack of a corresponding option in the current
FBIM. The result of T7 DK, based on only two measurements, indicates a larger traveling
speed, than the closest FBIM counterpart of T1, with a RE of 40.7%. The large difference
suggests, that the implementation of T7 DK might be sensible.
Time to Lay, Connect and Charge Hose - Table V
The activities to lay, connect, and charge hoses are considered a main activity in this project.
The nine activities in this category, included in experiment 2, are summarised in Table 4.11.
84
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:92==
They are all intended for implementation in FBIM, as there are currently no direct equivalent
counterparts available. For those possible, the closest counterparts are provided and used to
compare with the experimental results. The closest counterparts are in general those of Ø65
for B-hoses and Ø38 for C-hoses.
The activity of establishing the feeding line, i.e. to lay, connect and charge feeding line,
represented by V1 DK is compared with V1.2 from FBIM [3]. However, V1.2 also includes
the removal of the hose, which in this project is considered equivalent to P1. Thereby, some
discrepancy is expected. Despite this, the results in Table 4.11 and the acceptance criteria of
this project, indicate that the measurements of V1 DK indicatively validate V1.2, with a RE of
less than 20%. This is certainly unexpected and might suggest that V1.2 represents V1 DK
well enough. However, due to the difference in including the ’remove’-part, the activities are
considered too different to be definitively compared. It is noted, that the results of experiment 2,
based on 4 measurements, indicate a lower mean value. The small standard deviation compared
to the FBIM counterpart, might be due to the small sample pool.
The activity of establishing a main attack line, i.e. to lay, connect, and charge B-hoses, is
represented by V2.1 DK and V4.1 DK. Their closest FBIM counterparts are V2.1 and V4.1. The
same challenge with V1.2, is apparent for V2.1, as this also included the removal of the hose.
In this project, this part is considered represented by P4 DK or P6 DK, and some discrepancy
between V2.1 DK and V2.1 is therefore expected. The results in Table 4.11 indicate a RE of
above 30% and less than 30% for V2.1 DK and V4.1 DK, respectively. The acceptance criteria
of this project, suggest that V4.1 DK can be considered to indicatively validate V4.1, while
the RE is too large for V2.1 DK to indicatively validate V2.1. This suggests that V2.1 DK
and V2.1 are too different to be definitively compared, but also that there might only be a
small difference in charging a Ø65 hose compared to a B-hose. It is noted, that the results of
experiment 2, based on 4 measurements, indicate a higher mean value which suggests a slower
hose deployment. This might be due to the smaller hoses utilised in FBIM while contradicting
the fact that V2.1 includes the removal of the hoses. The small standard deviation compared
to the FBIM counterparts, might be due to the small sample pool.
The activity of establishing an attack branch, i.e. to lay, connect, and charge C-hoses, is
represented by V2.2 DK and V4.2 DK. Their closest FBIM counterparts are V2.2 and V4.2.
Again, due to the removal part of V2.2, some discrepancy between V2.2 and V2.2 DK is
expected. The removal is considered represented by P3 DK or P5 DK. The results in Table
4.11, indicate a RE of above 30% and less than 30% for V2.2 DK and V4.2 DK, respectively.
This suggests that this case is similar to the above, as V4.2 DK can be considered to indicatively
validate V4.2, while the RE is too large for V2.2 DK to indicatively validate V2.2. This supports
the above-mentioned observations, as the results suggest that V2.2 DK and V2.2 are too
different to be definitively compared and that there might only be a small difference in charging a
Ø38 hose compared to a C-hose. In both cases, the results indicate that the FBIM counterparts
are underestimating, as the obtained mean values are higher. Again, this might be due to the
smaller hoses utilised in FBIM, while contradicting the fact that V2.2 includes the removal of
the hoses. The small standard deviation compared to the FBIM counterparts, might be due to
the small sample pool.
The activities of V2.3 DK and V4.3 DK were outlined in this project, with the aim of assessing
the influence of a high-rise pack, and the time to establish such an attack line. However, the
results in Table 4.11 indicate much resemblance to those of V2.2 DK and V4.2 DK, and thus
the FBIM counterparts V2.2 and V4.2. This suggests that it might not be sensible to make
the distinction, as both activities could be represented by combined versions of V2.2 DK and
V4.2 DK.
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
85
==PAGE:93==
The activity of V6 DK was included in this project, to represent the laying and charging of
an HP hose, which is not represented in the FBIM dataset. Thereby, it was not possible to
simulate the fast deployment of an HP attack in an FBIM simulation. By the result in Table
4.11, the activity can now be accounted for in FBIM, but due to the very low sample pool, the
obtained result might not be representative of the activity.
Similarly, the activity of V7 DK was included in this project, to represent the feature of an
external hauling attack. It was intended to capture the whole operation of laying, hoisting,
and connecting the attack branch. However, it is unknown how the height of hoisting would
influence the duration of the activity. The result obtained in this project reflects a case of
hoisting to the second floor and consists of a single measurement. By the result in Table
4.11, the activity can now be accounted for in FBIM, but due to the very low sample pool,
the obtained result might not be representative of the activity, and limited for hoisting to the
second floor.
5.1.3
Experiment 3 - Search & Rescue Operation
The three activities represented by the results of experiment 3 are given in Tables 4.12 and
4.13. Two of the activities are intended for validation while one is intended for implementation.
Each activity and the results thereof are assessed in this section.
Time for Search and Rescue - Table Y
The activities of search and rescue are considered main activities in this project. The three
activities in this category, included in experiment 3, are summarised in Table 4.13. For the
activity of secondary search, represented by Y1, the obtained result seems to indicate a significant
discrepancy to the FBIM counterpart. By the acceptance criterion of this project, the activity
of Y1 can therefore not be considered as indicatively validated. The obtained results indicate a
mean search speed of twice the size, which might suggest that the FBIM counterpart is too
conservative.
The activity of Y1.2 DK was included in this project, intended for implementation and to
represent a secondary search performed with the aid of a thermal camera. The result in Table
4.13, suggests that the search speed is increased by almost 40% when aided by thermal cameras,
compared to the result of Y1. This is a significant result and indicates the need for this feature
to be incorporated into FBIM.
The results of both Y1 and Y1.2 DK are considered to significantly reflect the compartments,
in which the search operations were conducted. Therefore, these results might be limited in
mostly empty rooms and corridors with a max floor area of 50m2. This limit represents the
maximal floor area of the large compartment in Fig. 3.7. Thus, it might not be representative
of searches in very large compartments or heavily furnished compartments.
The last activity of experiment 3 is the activity to remove/rescue a person. It is represented by
Y2 and it is intended for validation. By the result in Table 4.13, the RE is above 70% which
indicates a large and significant discrepancy. It is, however, considered a more appropriate
representation of the activity than the one from experiment 1, as the activity was more realistic.
Thus, the result suggests that Y2 of FBIM might be too conservative. However, the activity to
remove/rescue a person depends on many circumstantial factors of the person e.g. injuries,
state of mind, and weight, as well as factors of the situation e.g. the person is being rescued
from rubble, from a basement, and how good the visibility is. The result of this experiment is
based on a training dummy of 70kg, which is easy to grab onto and carry out. It is considered
significantly more challenging to grab and carry out a real person, so this result might not
capture the full and realistic activity.
86
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:94==
Lastly, it is worth noting, that while experiment 3 was under development, it was assumed
that a search team could cover a width of 2.3m perpendicular to the search direction. By the
observations made during the experiment, this assumption is considered applicable and valid.
It is, however, considered to depend significantly on the experience and competencies of the
specific search team.
5.1.4

have been assessed with the goal to identify key indications. These indications have, in the

Tables 5.1 and 5.2.

5.2
Model Validation
In this section, the results obtained during the model validation of FBIM are assessed and
discussed. The results are based on a basic fire scenario, conducted as experiment 4 and
described in Section 3.2.4, from which a series of milestones were identified and documented.
These obtained milestones are then compared with those obtained by a corresponding FBIM
simulation. In fact, a series of three FBIM simulation cases have been devised, each reflecting
specific simulation assumptions. The fire scenario is inspired by the cases developed in [35]. It
is mainly focused on the main intervention phase, i.e. the main activities of establishing attack
lines and of search and rescue operations.
The milestones, and the associated results, are provided in Table 4.14. The results reflect the
duration of each milestone and are provided for both the experimental case and the three FBIM
simulation cases. The simulation cases are described in Section 3.5, and is documented in
Section 4.2, in the Tables 4.16, 4.15, and 4.17. The simulations are performed in the developed
program of this project, described in Section 3.4, enabling the cases to be visualised in a Gantt
88
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:96==
chart as in Fig. 4.1. The milestones are utilised as a measure of validation. This is done to
illuminate which parts of FBIM are indicated to accurately represent the given process, and
which parts fail to do so. Thereby, the model validation is more fundamental than simply
comparing the total duration of the intervention, which might be misleading. Thus, each
milestone is further assessed in the following, with the aim to validate FBIM in a Danish
context.
The model validation of FBIM, performed in this project, is highly case-sensitive. The results
reflect the specific scenario of experiment 4, and the corresponding FBIM simulations. Thus,
any chart, path, or input which have not been utilised in the simulations, can evidently not
be assessed. Furthermore, the specific fire scenario of experiment 4, is the sole measure of
validation. The validation is therefore highly dependent on this single intervention process.
Time for determination of fire location
This milestone is intended to represent the time from arrival to the fire scene, till the time
the fire is located. Thereby, it outlines the time until the OIC can deploy the SHTs. The fire
scenario began with the fire crew gathering near the appliance, already donning their safety
equipment. The OIC was greeted by a fire warden who communicated the fire location etc. at
which point the OIC could deploy the SHTs.
During experiment 4, this milestone was documented to have a duration of 16 seconds. The
milestone might be tainted by the fact that this was a training exercise i.e. the OIC possessed
some prior knowledge of the fire scenario, and the communication with the fire warden might
be too rehearsed. This suggests that for a real case, the duration might be longer. To describe
and simulate this milestone, FBIM Chart 5 is applied. By the specific path appropriate for
this fire scenario, the only contribution is from Box 10 and element H1. Table H reflects that
the time to communicate with the fire warden depends on the complexity and size of the
structure in question. In all the simulation cases in the project, the result for this milestone is
identical and provides a duration estimate of 30 seconds. Thereby, this yields a RE of 47%,
to the safe and conservative side. By the acceptance criterion of this project, this milestone
cannot be considered validated. However, it can be argued that the duration documented in
the experiment might not represent a real case.
Time to relay and receive orders
This milestone is intended to represent the time from the OIC is ready to deploy the SHTs, till
the time the SHT is ready to begin their primary tasks. Thereby, it outlines the time for the
OIC to relay orders, and for the crew to receive it. It is not a part of the current FBIM, but as
suggested in [35], the FBIM element C1 is assumed applicable to describe this activity.
In experiment 4, this milestone was documented to have a duration of 24 seconds. This
duration is assessed as valid to represent the activity in this specific fire scenario. However,
similar to Table H, it is considered that this activity might be dependent on factors such as the
complexity of the fire scenario, structure, and tasks. By the time documented in experiment 4,
the OIC had briefed the crew on the scenario and in their individual tasks, and they had had
their time for further elaboration. In all the simulation cases in the project, the result for this
milestone is identical and provides a duration estimate of 60 seconds. Thereby, this yields a
RE of 60%, to the safe and conservative side. By the acceptance criterion of this project, this
milestone cannot be considered validated. However, the selection of C1 has no empirical merit,
the activities were simply considered similar. The results of the experiment indicate that this
value is not directly applicable, but provides a conservative estimation.
Time to asses fire scene, OIC
When the OIC has initiated the crew, the fire scenario separates into several trajectories. This
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
89
==PAGE:97==
milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of the OIC, evaluating and assessing the fire
scene. This process is vital in the early requisitions of additional resources and in getting a
full grasp of the fire development. In this case, the milestone outlines the time for the OIC to
perform a reconnaissance of the scene by a perimeter walk.
This milestone was documented to have a duration of 49 seconds and is assessed as valid to
represent the activity. To describe and simulate this milestone, FBIM Chart 7 is applied. By the
specific path appropriate for this fire scenario, the only contribution is from Box 4 and element
Q1, along with a walking distance. To reflect the circumstances at which FBIM simulations are
expected to be performed, i.e. from behind a desk, the walking distance was roughly estimated
to be 79m. In simulation cases 1 and 2, the result for this milestone is identical and provides a
duration estimate of 34 −35 seconds. Thereby, this yields a RE of 40 −44% and indicates
an underestimation. The variation is simply due to the standard distributions in Python, with
limited pool size and no specific seed. It illustrates the sensitivity and accuracy of the developed
program, which could cause an error accumulation. However, for the purpose of this project,
this is not considered an issue. The underestimation of Q1 is also indicated by the findings
of experiment 2, as illustrated in Table 5.2. In simulation case 3 where Q1, collected in this
project, is utilised, a significant compliance is evident. By an underestimation with a RE of 4%,
simulation case 3 shows a great resemblance to the result documented in experiment 4.
Time to establish first attack line, SHT 1
This milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of SHT 1, establishing the first attack
line. It serves to outline the time it takes for SHT 1 to gather equipment, move to the set-up
area, and establish the attack line. In the fire scenario, SHT 1 had to pick up a high-rise pack
each, move to the set-up area on the platform and by the dry-pipe outlet, and lay out their
attack line. When charged, the nozzle was adjusted and they donned BA facemasks. The
milestone ends when SHT 1 opens the door, ready to advance.
The milestone was documented to have a duration of 195 seconds, or 3 minutes and 15 seconds.
The documented value is assessed as valid to represent the activity. To describe and simulate
this milestone, FBIM Chart 8 (with sub-Charts 6 and 9), and Chart 10, are applied. By the
specific path appropriate for this fire scenario, the contributions from Chart 8 constitute P2, Q2
(with 11m walking distance), and T1 (with 14 steps). The contributions from Chart 10 entail
V2.2 and V4.2 (with 45 mhl for each). Thereby, in simulation cases 1 and 2, the result for this
milestone is identical and provides a duration estimate of 112 −113 seconds. This yields a
RE of 73 −74% and indicates an underestimation. This supports the indications in Table 5.2.
By the acceptance criterion of this project, this milestone cannot be considered validated. In
simulation case 3 where appropriate data collected in this project, is utilised, the results seem
to have improved. However, the simulation still suggests a significant underestimation with a
RE of 39%. In all simulations, SHT 1 needed not to wait on FF 3, to establish water supply to
the dry-pipe system. This was also documented in the experiment.
Time to initiate fire control, SHT 1
This milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of SHT 1, reaching the fire compartment
and initiating fire control. It outlines the time it takes SHT 1 to locate and advance to the
fire compartment. In the fire scenario, the premises were smoke-logged, and the SHT had
been instructed to advance directly to the fire compartment. However, the OIC was instructed
to perform as they normally would, and thus, SHT 1 was permitted to ventilate when and if
possible, along the way. The milestone ends as SHT 1 has advanced to the fire compartment
and begins to apply water, i.e. initiate fire control.
The milestone was documented to have a duration of 201 seconds, or 3 minutes and 21 seconds.
The documented value is assessed as valid to represent the activity. To describe and simulate
90
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:98==
this milestone, FBIM sub-Chart 9 (within Chart 10), is applied. By the specific path appropriate
for this fire scenario, the contributions constitute Q3 (with 21m walking distance), and T5
(with 14 steps). Thereby, in simulation cases 1 and 2, the result for this milestone is identical
and provides a duration estimate of 29 seconds. This yields a RE of almost 600%, indicating a
significant underestimation. By the acceptance criterion of this project, this milestone cannot
be considered validated. In simulation case 3 where appropriate data collected in this project,
is utilised, the results seem to have worsened. These results suggest that FBIM cannot fully
capture the activity of firefighters making their way through smoke-logged compartments with
a charged C-hose, to reach the fire compartment. However, it can be argued that it may be
unreasonable to expect that the simple use of Q3 and T5 would describe this, as it, in this
case, also involves way-finding, opening windows, establishing hose depots, and communicating
with the OIC. In all cases, the simulations significantly underestimate this milestone which is
considered a clear indication that the model fails to represent the experiment.
Time to establish water supply, SHT 2
This milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of SHT 2, establishing the main attack
line and the feeding line. It serves to outline the time it takes for SHT 2 to gather equipment
and simultaneously establish the main attack line to the dry-pipe system, and a feeding line to
a hydrant. In the fire scenario, SHT 2 had to pick up their equipment, move to the end of the
appliance, and hand over a hose-end to the driver. The hoses would then be attached to the
appliance pump, while SHT 2 separated into FF 3, headed towards the dry-pipe inlet, and FF
4 headed towards the hydrant. When the respective hose-lines are charged, they have to be
reported back to OIC by the appliance. This milestone ends when SHT 2 is gathered near the
appliance, ready to be deployed.
The milestone was documented to have a duration of 95 seconds, or 1 minute and 35 seconds.
The documented value is assessed as valid to represent the activity. To describe and simulate
this milestone, FBIM Chart 8 (with sub-Chart 6), and Chart 10 (with sub-Chart 9), are applied
to each FF. By the specific paths appropriate for this fire scenario, the contributions to FF 3
constitute P1, V2.1, and V4.1 (with 2×15 mhl each) and O1, P1, V1.2 (with 30 mhl), and Q3
(with 30m walking distance). Thereby, in simulation cases 1 and 2, the result for this milestone
is identical and provides a duration estimate of 138−139 seconds. This yields a RE of 31−32%,
to the safe and conservative side. This supports the indications in Table 5.2. However, by
the acceptance criterion of this project, although close, this milestone cannot be considered
validated. In simulation case 3 where appropriate data, collected in this project, is utilised, the
results seem to have improved. By an underestimation with a RE of 11%, simulation case 3
illustrates a resemblance to the result documented in experiment 4. In all simulations and as
documented in the experiment, FF 3 had completed some time before FF 4, as indicated in
Fig. 4.1.
Time to establish second attack line, SHT 2
This milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of SHT 2, establishing the second attack
line. It serves to outline the time it takes for SHT 2 to establish the attack line and move to
the set-up area, ready to advance. In the fire scenario, SHT 2 had to roll out and lay 80m
Hp-hose before moving to the platform, on which a hose depot had to be established. When
charged, the nozzle was adjusted and they donned BA facemasks. The milestone ends when
SHT 2 opens the door, ready to begin the search and rescue operation.
The milestone was documented to have a duration of 155 seconds, or 2 minutes and 35 seconds.
The documented value is assessed as valid to represent the activity. To describe and simulate
this milestone, FBIM Chart 10 (with sub-Chart 9), is applied. However, in the case of an HP
attack, FBIM does not contain a value corresponding to this activity. Therefore, simulation
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
91
==PAGE:99==
case 1 assumes that V2.2 (and 80 mhl) is applicable while omitting the charging of the hose.
Simulation case 2 omits it completely, assuming walking speeds are enough, and simulation
case 3, utilises the data gathered during this project. The differences are documented in Tables
4.16, 4.15, and 4.17.
Simulation case 1 provides a duration estimate of 123 seconds. This yields a RE of 26% and
indicates an underestimation. Simulation case 2 provides a duration estimate of 34 seconds.
This yields a RE of 356% and indicates a significant underestimation. Simulation case 3
provides a duration estimate of 98 seconds. This yields a RE of 58% and also indicates
an underestimation. By the result of case 1 and the acceptance criterion of this project,
this milestone can be considered indicatively validated. However, this relies on the specific
assumption of case 1, which can be argued not to be appropriate. The results clearly indicate
that the assumption of case 2 cannot be justified, as they indicate a significant underestimate
of the duration. In case 3, contradicted the expectations, the results indicate a significant,
although smaller than in case 2, underestimation. It was, however, the expectation that the
implementation of V6 DK would enable a more accurate representation of the activity. The
results suggest that this has not been achieved.
The underestimation might be explained by the gear check the SHTs perform after donning
BA facemasks, which does not appear incorporated in FBIM. However, it is considered that
this activity won’t make up for the 32 sec in case 1 or the 57 sec in case 3. Another reason
might be the establishment of a hose depot on the platform before entry. This is not directly
incorporated in FBIM, but it is to some extent represented by the values of stair travel with
hoses, collected in this project. In any case, the implementation of HP hoses in Table V in
FBIM is considered necessary and advantageous. If, in case 1, the activity of V4.2 was included,
the duration estimate would have been extended by 49 seconds. Thereby, the total duration
of the milestone in case 1 would be 172 seconds, indicating a RE of 10%, to the safe and
conservative side. This is considered a significant indication, which might suggest, that an
HP hose could be represented by V2.2 and V4.2. However, as an HP attack is considered
notably different, the assumption to use of V2.2 and V4.2 does not seem reasonable, beyond
the indications of these results.
Time to initiate fire extinguishing, SHT 2
This milestone is intended to represent the trajectory of SHT 2, conducting search and rescue,
while advancing to the fire compartment. It serves to outline the time it takes for SHT 2 to
search the still smoke-logged premises. In the fire scenario, SHT 2 had to perform the search
equipped with an HP hose, and without a thermal camera. The milestone ends when the search
and rescue operation is complete (ending with the fire compartment), and SHT 2 begins to
apply water, i.e. initiate fire extinguishing.
The milestone was documented to have a duration of 347 seconds, or 5 minutes and 47 seconds.
The documented value is assessed as valid to represent the activity. During the experiment,
the search team was observed to perform the search in a way that could be characterised by
something in between primary and secondary search. This approach is considered a realistic
representation and might illustrate the challenge with the sharp distinction of Y1 and Q3.
Moreover, as before, SHT 2 was also permitted to ventilate along the way, increasing visibility
and lowering the heat, implying a faster search but a delay when opening windows, etc. To
describe and simulate this milestone, FBIM Chart 12 is applied. Simulation case 1 assumes that
secondary search, Y1 (and 100m2), is applicable while simulation case 2 assumes a primary
search, Q3 (and 65m), is applicable. Simulation case 3, utilises the result of secondary search
collected in experiment 3. The differences are documented in Tables 4.16, 4.15, and 4.17.
Thereby, simulation case 1 provides a duration estimate of 638 seconds. This yields a RE of
92
Firefighter Engineering - Validation of the Fire Brigade Intervention Model in Danish Fire Safety Design
==PAGE:100==
46% to the safe and conservative side. Simulation case 2 provides a duration estimate of 61
seconds. This yields a RE of 469% and indicates a significant underestimation. Simulation case
3 provides a duration estimate of 364 seconds. Thus, the result indicates significant compliance.
By an underestimation with a RE of 5%, simulation case 3 shows great resemblance to the
result documented in experiment 4. By the result of case 1 and the acceptance criterion of this
project, this milestone cannot be considered validated. Moreover, the results clearly indicate
that the assumption of case 2 cannot be justified, as it yields an unreasonable underestimate
of the duration. These results suggest that Y1 is more appropriate than Q3, to describe this
activity. In case 3 the results indicate a significant compliance, which suggests that Y1 in
FBIM is conservative. It should be noted that Y1 obtained in experiment 3, is expected to
reflect the topology, etc. of the compartments in question. The fact that experiments 3 and 4
were conducted in the same building, might explain the significant resemblance. However, this
suggests that the Y1, obtained in experiment 3, might fail to represent a different topology.
